Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Change of Rulings with Change of Custom
Explaining how many rulings change with changing customs due to corruption or necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that rulings change with changing times to identify when later positions supersede earlier custom-based rulings.

Core Idea
Rulings based on custom change when custom changes; later fatwa may differ from the Imam's original view.

This passage introduces a foundational principle of Hanafi fatwa methodology: rulings that rely on customary practice (urf) are subject to change when the underlying custom changes. It explains why later Hanafi scholars adopted positions different from Abu Hanifa's on hiring for Quran teaching, witness investigation, and duress — not because the evidence changed, but because the era changed.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Identify the Arabic maxim and its translation. Understand that it applies to rulings based on custom, not definitive texts.
2
Identify the three examples given
List the three cases: hiring for Quran teaching, witness investigation, and duress from non-rulers. Note the original position of Abu Hanifa and the later position adopted by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
3
For each example, explain why the ruling changed
Connect the change to a specific change in custom: negligence in religious duties (Quran), corruption in society (witnesses), rise of powerful non-rulers (duress).
4
Understand the concept of ikhtilaf 'asr wa zaman
The difference is one of era, not evidence. This reconciles the apparent contradiction without accusing earlier scholars of error.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative fatwa) today
In all three examples, the fatwa follows the later position (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because it suits contemporary reality.

Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, said: He must ask about them privately and publicly in all claims... It was said: This is a difference of time and era, and the fatwa is on their statement in this time.' (al-Hidayah with Fath al-Qadir, 6/458, print: Kunta) (3) His saying: 'The realization of duress from other than the ruler' – Al-Tanwir and its commentary: 'Its conditions are four: the ability of the one compelling to inflict what he threatened, whether ruler, thief, or similar.' And said in Al-Radd al-Muhtar: 'His saying: "ruler or thief" – this is according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). They said: This is a difference of time and era, not a difference of proof and evidence; because in his (Abu Hanifa's) time there was no force from other than the ruler with which duress could be realized, so he answered based on what he observed. In their time, corruption emerged, and the matter fell to every overpowering person, so duress is realized from anyone, and the fatwa is on their statement. As in...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents a key principle in Hanafi fatwa methodology: rulings based on custom may change with changing times. The opening statement declares that many rulings of the founding mujtahid (Abu Hanifa) have changed due to corruption of the era or general necessity, even though they contradict his explicit text. Three examples follow, each from the footnotes. First: hiring for teaching the Quran (isti'jar). Abu Hanifa's original position likely prohibited taking wages for teaching Quran, but later scholars permitted it to prevent neglect of Quran memorization. Second: not being satisfied with apparent justice of witnesses. Abu Hanifa relied on the apparent Islamic character of a witness, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad required investigation. The text notes this is a difference of time, and fatwa is now on the latter view. Third: duress from non-sultan (e.g., a thief or strongman). Abu Hanifa only recognized duress from the ruler because in his time no one else had such power. Later, conditions changed, and duress can come from anyone capable of carrying out the threat. Fatwa follows Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The key takeaway: these changes are not arbitrary; they are based on the principle that custom changes, and the later position is adopted to avoid hardship and suit contemporary reality.

Key Terms
تغير الأحكام (taghayyur al-ahkam): The change of legal rulings due to change in custom, time, or condition.
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Duress; compulsion by threat of harm, legally recognized when the coercer has the ability to carry out the threat.
اختلاف عصر و زمان (ikhtilaf 'asr wa zaman): A difference of era and time, not a difference of evidence; used to reconcile conflicting rulings from different periods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did later Hanafi scholars permit hiring for teaching the Quran despite Abu Hanifa's apparent prohibition?
A) They considered Abu Hanifa's original ruling to be mistaken.
B) The custom and necessity changed; people became negligent, so hiring was allowed to preserve Quran memorization.
C) A new hadith was discovered that permitted it.
D) They followed the Shafi'i school in this matter.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle 'rulings change with changing times' allows the school to adapt custom-based rulings without compromising its foundational evidence.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate

Change of Rulings with Change of Custom: Fatwa Examples
Listing examples of fatwa rulings changed due to custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle 'taghayyur al-ahkam bi taghayyur al-urf' by analyzing each fatwa example to identify the original ruling, the new fatwa, and the rationale based on changed customs or fasad al-zaman.

Core Idea
Rulings change when customs change — a mufti must adapt to protect people's interests in a corrupt era.

This lesson presents a series of fatwa examples collected by Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar, illustrating how later Hanafi muftis deviated from the original madhhab (Zahir al-Riwayah) due to changing social conditions and widespread corruption. Each example shows the interplay between the fixed text and the dynamic context, a core skill for any mufti. You will work through these cases to understand not just the rulings but the legal reasoning behind the change.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original madhhab ruling
For each example, first determine what the original Hanafi position was according to Zahir al-Riwayah. The passage usually states the original view explicitly (e.g., 'contrary to the original madhhab of no liability').
The original ruling is the starting point; the change is a deviation justified by custom.
2
State the later fatwa ruling
Next, identify the new ruling that the later muftis adopted. This is what they 'gave fatwa upon' (أفتوا به). Often it is the opposite of the original.
The fatwa ruling is the operative position for practical application.
3
Identify the reason for change
Determine the justification provided: either 'change of custom' (تغير العرف) or 'corruption of the times' (فساد الزمان). Sometimes both. The commentaries in Radd al-Muhtar explain the rationale.
The reason must be genuine; arbitrary changes are not allowed.
4
Assess the authority weight
Recognize that the later fatwa, though contrary to Zahir al-Riwayah, becomes the mufta bihi (relied-upon position) due to necessity. Understand the hierarchy: original text vs. contextual fatwa.
This is a key lesson in Rasm al-Mufti: when and how to depart from the transmitted text.

And their preventing the judge from judging by his own knowledge. (1)
Change of rulings due to change of custom.
- And their fatwa preventing the husband from traveling with his wife, even if he gave her the prompt dower; due to corruption of the times. (2)
9 - And not accepting his statement: that he made an exception after swearing to divorce her, except with evidence, even though it is contrary to the apparent narration, and they explained it by corruption of the times. (3)
10 - And not believing her after consummation (4) that she did not receive what was stipulated as prompt dower, even though she denies receipt, and the rule of the madhhab is that the statement is for the denier, but usually she does not submit herself before receiving it.
11 - And likewise they said regarding his statement: "every lawful upon unlawful" results in divorce.
(1) His statement: their preventing the judge from judging by his own knowledge. He said in Radd al-Muhtar: This is based on the fact that the judge may act on his knowledge, and fatwa is against it in our time, as quoted in al-Ashbah from Jami' al-Fusulayn, and it is restricted to our time due to corruption of judges; the original madhhab is permissibility. (423/5)
(2) His statement: corruption of the people of the time. He said in Radd al-Muhtar: then he mentioned from the two jurists - Abu Qasim al-Saffar and Abu al-Layth - that he does not have the right to travel at all without her consent due to corruption of the times; because she is not safe for herself in her house, so how if she goes out? and he stated in al-Mukhtar that fatwa is upon it. (146/3)
(3) His statement: and they explained it by corruption of the times. He said in al-Tanwir and its commentary: and his statement is accepted if he claims it, and she denies it, in the apparent narration from the school's founder. It is said: it is not accepted except with evidence, and reliance and fatwa are upon it out of caution due to prevalence of corruption. (Radd al-Muhtar: 370/3)
(4) His statement: and not believing her after consummation. He said in Radd al-Muhtar: I say: the summary is that if the woman's husband dies and he has consummated with her, and she comes demanding her dower, or her heirs after her death, and the custom is that she does not submit herself except after receiving something of the dower, like one hundred dirhams for example, she is not adjudged all of the proper dower when no specification is made; rather it is considered: if she acknowledges what she received according to custom, otherwise she is adjudged against it.
(Radd al-Muhtar: 151/3, al-Bahr al-Raiq: 321/3)
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a detailed illustration of the rasm rule 'Change of rulings due to change of custom' (تغير الأحكام بتغير العرف). It provides numerous examples from Hanafi fatwa practice where later muftis issued rulings contrary to the original madhhab (Zahir al-Riwayah) due to changing circumstances and corruption of the times (فساد الزمان). The author, Ibn Abidin, is compiling these from Radd al-Muhtar and other sources. Each example shows a departure from the original ruling for a reason related to custom or societal change. For instance, making the hired worker liable (تضمين الأجير المشترك) was accepted in fatwa because people had become dishonest. The guardian's prohibition from mudaraba with orphan's wealth was to protect the orphan's property. Making the usurper of real estate liable and limiting lease periods for waqf properties were precautions against loss in a corrupt era. Preventing the judge from judging by his own knowledge was due to the corruption of judges. Restricting the husband's travel with his wife without consent, not accepting an exception claim after a divorce oath without evidence, and not believing the wife's claim of non-receipt of dower after consummation—all these were justified by the corruption of the times or established custom. Finally, the phrase 'كل حل على حرام' causing divorce refers to a specific legal maxim. The passage emphasizes that a mufti must consider the context and prevailing conditions, and cannot blindly follow the textual rulings when they lead to harm or injustice.

Key Terms
تغير الأحكام بتغير العرف (taghayyur al-ahkam bi taghayyur al-urf): A principle that legal rulings may change when customs and social conditions change, especially in non-ritual matters.
فساد الزمان (fasad al-zaman): Corruption of the times: a justification for deviating from original rulings due to widespread moral decay or dishonesty.
أصل المذهب (asl al-madhhab): The original ruling of the madhhab as transmitted in the authoritative texts (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the Hanafi muftis of later times issue a fatwa that the guardian cannot do mudaraba with the orphan's wealth?
A) Because Imam Abu Hanifa explicitly forbade it.
B) Because the original ruling was for a different time and circumstances changed, making it risky for the orphan's wealth.
C) Because mudaraba is invalid in general.
D) Because guardians are always dishonest.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle 'rulings change with changing customs' allows muftis to adapt fatwas to new circumstances while remaining within the school's methodology, provided the change is justified by genuine custom or necessity.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Rulings Change with Changing Customs: The Mufti's Authority
Explains a rasm al-mufti rule allowing departure from mansus due to new custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-ahkam tataghayyar bi taghayyur al-zaman' to identify when a mufti may depart from the mansus (explicit text) in Zahir al-Riwaya.

Core Idea
A mufti can depart from the explicit text if a new custom arises, guided by sound reasoning.

The Hanafi school is known for its robust use of custom ('urf) as a subsidiary source of law. This lesson presents a foundational rasm al-mufti rule: rulings can change with changing times and customs. The passage from Sharh al-Uqood explains the rationale, provides concrete examples, and clarifies that this is not an arbitrary departure but a controlled mechanism within the madhhab. You will learn how the later Hanafi jurists justified contradicting explicit rulings from the founding imams when custom had shifted.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Identify the qawida: 'al-ahkam tataghayyar bi taghayyur al-zaman' (rulings change with changing times). Recognize that it applies primarily to mu'amalat (transactions) and not acts of worship (ibadat), which are tawqifi.
The maxim is a wusta (intermediate) legal maxim.
2
Examine the examples in the passage
Work through the three examples: (1) drinking from a water skin without specifying quantity, (2) entering a bathhouse without stating duration or water usage, (3) borrowing dough or bread without weighing. Note that all these would normally be problematic due to uncertainty (gharar), but custom validates them.
These examples show custom overriding the general prohibition of gharar.
3
Understand the justification for change
The passage argues that if the Imam (e.g., Abu Hanifa) were alive at the time of the later jurists, he would have ruled according to the new custom. This counterfactual reasoning legitimizes departing from the mansus (explicit text) in Zahir al-Riwaya.
This is based on the principle that rulings are built on the custom of the time.
4
Identify the condition: sound reasoning (nazar sahih)
The mufti may only contradict the explicit text if he has sound reasoning (nazar sahih). This is not a license for arbitrary change but a disciplined process within the madhhab.
The passage emphasizes that later scholars only did so due to a new custom.
5
Apply the exception for ibadat
Note the istithna': acts of worship are not subject to change by custom. Use the exception to test your understanding: if a new custom arises for prayer timings, it cannot change the ruling because ibadat are tawqifi.
The governing qawida for ibadat is: al-asl fi al-ibadat al-hazr.

Change of rulings with change of custom. 21 - Drinking from a water skin without stating the quantity of what is drunk. 22 - Entering the bathhouse without stating the duration of stay and the amount of water poured. 23 - Borrowing dough and bread without weighing. And other things built upon custom. Many such issues have been mentioned in al-Ashbah. All these have had their rulings changed due to change of time, either due to necessity, or custom, or circumstantial evidence. And all that is not outside the madhhab; because if the founder of the madhhab were in this time, he would have ruled accordingly, and if this change had occurred in his time, he would not have stated the contrary. This is what emboldened the mujtahids of the madhhab and those of sound insight among the later scholars to contradict what is explicitly stated from the founder of the madhhab in the books of Zahir al-Riwaya, based on what was in his time, as their explicit statements have passed in the issue of 'every (marriage) is forbidden' that Muhammad built his statement on the custom of his time, and likewise what we presented on hiring for teaching. Section: It is permissible for the mufti to contradict the explicit text and follow the new custom if he has sound reasoning. If you say: Custom changes time after time, so if another custom arises that did not occur in the previous time, is it permissible for the mufti to contradict the explicit text and follow the new custom? I say: Yes! Indeed, the later scholars who contradicted the explicit text in the past issues did not contradict it except due to the occurrence of a custom after the time of the Imam, so the mufti may follow his new custom. (1) His saying: 'or circumstantial evidence' — qarina in terminology is something that points to the desired. Al-Sarakhsi said in Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir: If a Muslim has wine with him and says, 'I intend to make vinegar' or 'it is not mine,' if he is trustworthy, he is released because his apparent situation testifies for him, and building on the apparent is obligatory until its contrary becomes clear. And likewise other issues where they acted upon custom and circumstantial evidence, and they treated that as speech and sufficed with circumstantial evidence for explicit statement, and to that points the verse 'Indeed in that are signs for those who discern' and the verse 'And a witness from her family testified' ... end quote from Ibn Abidin's treatises 2/128.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule on the authority of custom in changing rulings. The author presents the principle 'taghayyur al-ahkam bi taghayyur al-zaman' (rulings change with changing times) and supports it with examples: drinking from a waterskin without specifying quantity (allowed because of custom), entering a bathhouse without specifying duration (by custom), and borrowing dough or bread without weighing (by custom, despite the general prohibition of gharar). The passage emphasizes that such changes are not outside the Hanafi madhhab because the Imam himself would have ruled differently if he lived in later times with new customs. This reasoning emboldened later mujtahids to contradict the explicit rulings (mansus) of the founding imams found in Zahir al-Riwaya texts. The author then explicitly states the rule: it is permissible for the mufti to contradict the explicit text and follow a new custom, provided he has sound reasoning (nazar sahih). A hypothetical objection is raised: if customs keep changing, can a mufti always follow the new custom? The answer is yes, because the later scholars only contradicted the explicit text due to new customs. The passage concludes with a definition of 'qarina' (circumstantial evidence) from al-Sarakhsi, illustrating how actions and circumstances can take the place of explicit speech, as shown in the Quranic stories of Yusuf and Sulayman. This principle gives the mufti significant flexibility while remaining within the madhhab framework.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Custom or habitual practice of people that is not explicitly prohibited by Shariah.
المنصوص (al-mansus): The explicitly stated ruling from the Imam (e.g., Abu Hanifa) in the canonical books of the madhhab.
القرينة (al-qarina): Circumstantial evidence that indicates a desired meaning or fact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when is it permissible for a mufti to contradict an explicit ruling (mansus) from the Imam?
A) Whenever he feels it is appropriate.
B) Only if a new custom has arisen that changes the underlying circumstances.
C) Only if the ruling is from Nawadir and not Zahir al-Riwaya.
D) Never, as the Imam's rulings are binding for all time.

Key Takeaway
The mufti may depart from the explicit text (mansus) if custom has changed and he has sound reasoning, within the Hanafi madhhab framework.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate

Three Conditions for Following a New Custom
Stating three conditions for a mufti to follow a new custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to IDENTIFY the three conditions for a mufti to follow an emerging custom and APPLY them to evaluate whether a contemporary custom may be adopted in a fatwa.

Core Idea
A mufti cannot blindly follow old texts; he must know his time's custom, have ijtihad, and train under a master.

This lesson presents three essential conditions for a mufti to legitimately adopt a new custom (al-urf al-hadith) in his fatwas. The Hanafi school does not permit static adherence to the literal texts when custom has changed; rather, the mufti must exercise informed judgment, consider public interest, and have proper training. We will examine each condition and the supporting authorities from our source text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Read the passage and list the three conditions explicitly mentioned for the mufti to follow a new custom. Note that each condition builds on the previous one.
2
Understand the rationale for each condition
For each condition, explain why it is necessary. Consider the absence of ijtihad in later times, the need to distinguish valid custom from invalid, and the role of apprenticeship in mastering fiqh terminology.
3
Analyze the supporting authorities
The passage cites Munyat al-Mufti, al-Qunyah, al-Ashbah, and al-Bazzaziyyah. Examine what each source adds to the argument, especially the statement that the mufti must not rule against the custom of his time.
4
Apply the exception
Note the exception: a custom may not be adopted if it contradicts a definitive text (nass). Understand that custom operates within the bounds of Shariah and cannot override clear rulings.
5
Evaluate the qasama example
Read the example from the chapter of qasama where Imam Abu Hanifa's view was set aside due to changed custom and maslahah. Identify how the three conditions justify this change.

The emerging custom: verbal customs, and similarly in the rulings that the mujtahid built upon what was in the custom of his time, and his custom changed to another custom, following them, but only after the mufti is someone who has sound opinion and insight, and knowledge of the principles of Shariah so that he can distinguish between a custom upon which rulings may be built and other customs; for the earlier scholars stipulated ijtihad in the mufti, and this is absent in our time, so at the least it is required that he have knowledge of the issues with their conditions and restrictions which they often omit and do not mention explicitly, relying on the understanding of the student of fiqh. Likewise, he must know the custom of his time and the conditions of its people, and be trained in that under a skilled teacher. Hence it is said at the end of 'Munyat al-Mufti': 'If a man memorized all...' (1) His statement: 'But after' is in the three copies we have, and perhaps this word is 'it is necessary' by the context of his later statement: 'Likewise it is necessary...' And this is a restriction of the permissibility for the mufti to follow the emerging custom with three conditions: First: that he have a degree of ijtihad and knowledge of the principles of Shariah and the branches that derive from them with their restrictions and conditions that the jurists omit relying on the understanding of the student of fiqh. He said in Radd al-Muhtar in the discussion of the obligatory acts of prayer: 'Al-Rahmati said and he gave absolute statements relying on what has preceded and what will come, as is their habit in giving absolute statements relying on the qualification in its proper place.' He said in al-Bahr: 'Their intention in that is that no one should claim knowledge of their science except one who has competed with them in it, and that it may be known that it is not acquired except by frequent review, tracing their expressions, and taking from the scholars, so understand.' (1) (450) Second: that he know the conditions of the people of his time and have a firm observation of their customs and transactions; for whoever is ignorant of the people of his time is ignorant. Third: that he be trained in fatwa under a skilled teacher; for studying fiqh and learning it, even memorizing fiqh branches, does not permit a scholar to rush to give fatwa until he trains for ifta under a skilled teacher, and he testifies to his qualification, and permits him to give fatwa or judge. This is because, as previously mentioned, the jurists have a special style and particular terminologies which must be examined to understand their true intent, and that is not acquired except by taking fiqh from its people, and having a psychological faculty in it such that his correctness overcomes his error. [PAGE_187] Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti 187 The emerging custom: The books of our scholars state: 'He must apprentice for fatwa so that he may be guided to it; because many issues are answered according to the customs of the people of the time in matters that do not violate Shariah.' End quote. And in al-Qunyah: 'It is not for the mufti nor for the judge to judge according to the apparent (1) madhhab and leave the custom.' End quote. And it is transmitted from it in Khizanat al-Riwayat. This is explicit in what we said: that the mufti does not give fatwa contrary to the custom of the people of his time. And close to it is what is transmitted in al-Ashbah from al-Bazzaziyyah: that the mufti gives fatwa according to what he perceives as maslahah (public interest) (2). (2) And I wrote in Radd al-Muhtar in the chapter of Qasama regarding the case where a guardian makes a claim against a man not from the inhabitants of the district, and two of them testify against him, it is not accepted according to him (Abu Hanifa) but according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is accepted... etc.: 'Al-Sayyid al-Hamawi transmitted from al-Allamah al-Maqdisi that he said: I refrained from giving fatwa according to the statement of the Imam and forbade its dissemination because of the general harm resulting from it, for the defiant among the people become bold to kill in districts free of...' (1) His statement: 'It is not for the mufti nor for the judge to judge according to the apparent madhhab' i.e., in matters based on custom, such as verbal expressions whose customary meaning people have come to know, and absolute contracts that they qualify with restrictions they do not mention explicitly. And his statement: 'The mufti does not give fatwa contrary to the custom of the people of his time' i.e., in the matters that have been narrated from the scholars of the madhhab and the custom has changed and the condition of the people of the time has altered. (2) His statement: 'The mufti gives fatwa according to what he perceives as maslahah' i.e., he does not give fatwa contrary to maslahah considered by Shariah and reason, nor contrary to the welfare of the general public in their religion and worldly affairs. Similarly, when statements conflict before him and he is unable to weigh, he gives fatwa according to the statement that contains the people's interest. See its example in al-Durr al-Mukhtar in the chapter of Mahr when discussing travel with the wife (3/147). Likewise, if he observes the people's necessity and need, and senses that removing them from their prevailing widespread custom would cause hardship, he gives fatwa according to what is more suitable and beneficial for them, even if it requires specifying a text or abandoning qiyas; but if it requires abandoning a text, then no; and its detailed explanation will come.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti addresses a critical issue in Hanafi fatwa methodology: how to handle emerging customs (al-urf al-hadith). The author lays down three conditions for a mufti to follow a new custom. First, the mufti must have a degree of ijtihad and thorough knowledge of the principles of Shariah and the subsidiary rulings with all their conditions and restrictions, which earlier jurists often omitted relying on the reader's understanding. This is because the foundational scholars (muttaqaddimun) required ijtihad in a mufti, but in later times this is absent, so at least he must have deep expertise. Second, he must be well-versed in the customs and conditions of the people of his time; ignorance of the people's customs amounts to ignorance. Third, he must train under a skilled teacher (ustadh mahir) and receive permission for fatwa, because fiqh has a specialized style and terminology that can only be mastered by studying under experts. The passage then cites authorities like Munyat al-Mufti, al-Qunyah, al-Ashbah, and al-Bazzaziyyah to emphasize that the mufti and judge must not blindly adhere to the apparent wording of the madhhab when custom has changed; they must consider the actual custom and maslahah. The commentary in the footnotes clarifies that this applies only to rulings built on custom, such as interpretation of words and absolute contracts. The mufti may even give preference to a view that serves maslahah over a stronger textual indication, provided he does not outright abandon a definitive text (nass). This reflects the Hanafi principle of istihsan and consideration of changed circumstances. The example from the chapter of Qasama shows how a ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa that would cause general harm was abandoned by later scholars due to changed social conditions.

Key Terms
العرف الحادث (al-urf al-hadith): A new custom that emerges after the time of the early jurists, which the contemporary mufti may consider in his fatwas, provided certain conditions are met.
المفتى (al-mufti): The person authorized to issue fatwas. In this context, a mufti must have a degree of ijtihad or at least deep knowledge of rules, current customs, and training under a master.
التخرج (al-takharrurj): Training under a skilled scholar to acquire the competence for fatwa, often resulting in formal permission (ijazah).
المصلحة (al-maslahah): Public interest or benefit that the mufti considers when giving fatwa, especially when navigating conflicting opinions or changed circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the second condition required for a mufti to follow a new custom?
A) Knowledge of the Arabic language
B) Knowledge of the conditions of the people of his time and their customs
C) Memorization of all fiqh branches
D) Following the apparent madhhab literally

Key Takeaway
A mufti may adopt a new custom only if he has sufficient expertise, knowledge of his society, and proper training; and it must not contradict a definitive text.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate

The Mufti and Custom: When Custom Overrides the Apparent Madhhab
Explains rule that custom overrides apparent madhhab ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the mufti should give precedence to prevalent custom over the apparent ruling of the madhhab, using the examples of qasamah testimony and eating during fasting.

Core Idea
Custom can override the apparent madhhab ruling when it serves public interest and does not oppose Shariah.

This lesson addresses a critical aspect of the mufti's craft: balancing textual authority with lived reality. The classical Hanafi texts may yield a ruling that, due to changed circumstances, leads to widespread harm or hardship. In such cases, as this passage shows, the mufti is not merely a transmitter of static rulings but an active jurist who weighs custom and public welfare. Through two concrete examples—testimony in qasamah and eating meat stuck between teeth during a fast—you will see how later Hanafi masters prioritized custom and maslahah over the apparent position of the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the governing maxim
State the maxim from al-Qunyah: 'It is not for the mufti or the judge to rule according to the apparent [text] of the madhhab and leave aside custom.' Understand that this is a binding rule for issuing fatwas.
This maxim is a dabt (restrictive sub-maxim) under the broader qawida 'al-ʿādah muḥakkamah'.
2
Analyze the justification
The passage clarifies that many issues are answered according to the customs of the people of the time, as long as they do not oppose the Shariah. This shows that custom is a valid source for determining the operative ruling, even if it diverges from the textual position.
The phrase 'fīmā lā yukhalif al-sharīʿah' sets the boundary: custom cannot override an explicit divine text.
3
Work through the qasamah example
Consider the case: a guardian from a neighborhood testifies against an outsider. Abu Hanifah rejects such testimony; Sahibayn accept it. Later scholars stopped giving fatwa on Abu Hanifah's view because it led to harm (evil people killing with impunity). They adopted Sahibayn's view.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is clear: the ruling shifts to prevent general harm.
4
Work through the fasting example
When a fasting person eats meat stuck between his teeth, Abu Hanifah distinguishes between abundant and scanty; Zufar says it always breaks the fast. The mufti must look at the person's nature: if the person finds it repulsive, he follows Abu Yusuf (no kaffarah); otherwise, Zufar (kaffarah). Custom and individual condition determine the fatwa.
This shows that even within ibadat, custom and personal circumstances can influence which opinion to adopt.
5
Synthesize the mufti's approach
Combine the lessons: when there are multiple narrations without clear preference, the mufti acts on considerations of changing custom, people's conditions, what is more beneficial, what has become practice, and what is stronger in evidence. He must distinguish these in reality, not by mere opinion. If he cannot, he refers to someone who can.
This is a fatwa methodology, not just a theoretical rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العرف الحادث
كتب أصحابنا لا بد أن يتلمّذ للفتوى، حتى يهتدى إليه ؛ لأن كثيراً من المسائل يجاب عنه على عادات أهل الزمان فيما لا يخالف الشريعة" . اهـ . وفى "القنية" : "ليس للمفتى ولا للقاضي أن يحكما على ظاهر المذهب ، ويتركا العرف" . اهـ . ونقله منها في خزانة الروايات ". وهذا صريح فيما قلنا من أن المفتى لا يفتى بخلاف عرف أهل زمانه . ويقرب منه ما نقله في "الأشباه عن البزازية " من أن المفتى يفتي بما يقع عنده من المصلحة .
وكتبت في رد المحتار في باب القسامة فيما لو ادعى الولى على رجل من غير أهل المحلة، وشهد اثنان منهم عليه، لم تقبل عنده وقالا تقبل . . . إلخ : "نقل السيد الحموى عن العلامة المقدسي أنه قال : توقفت عن الفتوى بقول الإمام ومنعت من إشاعته ؛ لما يترتب عليه من الضرر العام، فإن من عرفه من المتمردين يتجاسر على قتل النفس فى المحلات الخالية من غير أهلها، معتمداً على عدم قبول شهادتهم عليه ، حتى قلت : ينبغي الفتوى على قولهما، لا سيما والأحكام تختلف باختلاف الأيام "
وقال في "فتح القدير " في باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة من كتاب الصوم عند قول "الهداية " : " ولو أكل لحما بين أسنانه لم يفطر، وإن كان كثيراً يفطر، وقال زفر : يفطر في الوجهين " . اهـ . ما نصه : " والتحقيق أن المفتى في الوقائع لا بد له من ضرب اجتهاد ومعرفة بأحوال الناس، وقد عرف أن الكفارة تفتقر إلى كمال الجناية، فينظر إلى صاحب الواقعة : إن كان ممن يعاف طبعه ذلك ، أخذ بقول أبي يوسف، وإن كان ممن لا أثر لذلك عنده أخذ بقول زفر ) . اهـ . وفى تصحيح العلامة قاسم " : "فإن قلت : قد يحكون أقوالا من غير ترجیح ، وقد يختلفون في التصحيح؟ قلت : يعمل بمثل ما عملوا من اعتبار تغيّر العرف ، وأحوال الناس، وما هو الأرفق بالناس، وما ظهر عليه التعامل، وما قوى وجهه، ولا يخلو الوجود من تمييز هذا حقيقة لا ظنّا بنفسه ، ويرجع من لم يميز إلى من يميز البراءة ذمته " . اهـ .
The emergent custom. Our scholars wrote: 'It is necessary that one study for fatwa, so that he is guided to it, because many issues are answered according to the customs of the people of the time, in what does not oppose the Shariah.' And in al-Qunyah: 'It is not for the mufti or the judge to rule according to the apparent [text] of the madhhab and leave aside custom.' And he narrated it from there in Khizanat al-Riwayat. This is explicit in what we said: that the mufti does not give a fatwa contrary to the custom of the people of his time. Close to it is what was narrated in al-Ashbah from al-Bazzaziyyah: that the mufti gives fatwa according to what appears to him of maslahah (public interest). And I wrote in Radd al-Muhtar, in the chapter of qasamah, regarding the case: if the guardian claims against a man from outside the neighborhood, and two of them testify against him, it is not accepted according to Abu Hanifah but is accepted according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Then he said: 'Al-Sayyid al-Hamawi narrated from al-Allamah al-Maqdisi that he said: I refrained from giving fatwa according to the statement of the Imam and forbade its dissemination due to the general harm that follows from it, for those who know it from the rebellious ones become bold to kill people in empty neighborhoods, relying on the non-acceptance of testimony against them, until I said: It is appropriate to give fatwa according to their statement, especially since rulings differ with the difference of days.' And he said in Fath al-Qadir, in the chapter on what necessitates qada' and kaffarah from the book of fasting, at the statement of al-Hidayah: 'If someone ate meat between his teeth, he does not break his fast, even if it is abundant, he breaks his fast; and Zufar said: He breaks his fast in both cases.' The text: 'The investigation is that the mufti, in incidents, must have a measure of ijtihad and knowledge of the conditions of people, and it is known that kaffarah requires completion of the offense. So he looks at the person of the incident: if he is one whose nature abhors that, he takes the statement of Abu Yusuf; and if he is one who has no effect from that, he takes the statement of Zufar.' And in the Tashih of Allamah Qasim: 'If you say: They may narrate statements without preference, and they may differ in tashih? I say: He acts according to what they acted upon: consideration of change of custom, conditions of people, what is more beneficial for people, what has become the practice, and what is stronger in its aspect. And existence is not devoid of one who distinguishes this in reality, not by opinion. And whoever cannot distinguish should refer to one who can distinguish, for his immunity.'
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage addresses a key rasm al-mufti rule: the mufti must consider contemporary custom ('urf) and public interest (maslahah) when issuing fatwas. It opens by citing the principle that many rulings are answered according to the customs of the time, provided they do not contradict Shariah. Al-Qunyah sharply states that neither a mufti nor a judge should rule based on the apparent text of the madhhab and discard custom—indicating that custom can override the apparent ruling in matters built upon custom. The author confirms that the mufti must not give fatwa contrary to the custom of his era. He then supports this with a similar ruling from al-Ashbah: the mufti acts on what appears to him as maslahah. Two concrete examples follow. First, from Radd al-Muhtar on qasamah (oath and testimony): Abu Hanifah did not accept testimony from residents against a non-resident, but the two (Sahibayn) did. Given widespread harm (mafsadah) in times where people might exploit this ruling, the later scholar al-Maqdisi refrained from giving fatwa on Abu Hanifah's position and recommended fatwa on the Sahibayn's view, emphasizing that rulings change with changing times. Second, from Fath al-Qadir on fasting: if someone eats meat stuck between his teeth, Abu Hanifah distinguishes between a small amount (no break) and a large amount (break), while Zufar says it breaks the fast in both cases. The commentary explains the mufti must have ijtihad and knowledge of people's conditions. If the person's nature would abhor such meat (i.e., find it repulsive), then Abu Yusuf's view applies (that no kaffarah is due because the offense is incomplete). If the person does not mind, Zufar's view applies (kaffarah due). This shows that custom and individual circumstances affect which ruling to adopt. Finally, Allamah Qasim's Tashih clarifies that when scholars transmit multiple views without preference, the mufti should act on the same considerations as they did: change of custom, people's conditions, what is more beneficial, what has become common practice, and what is stronger in evidence. The mufti must be able to distinguish these in reality, not mere opinion; those who cannot must refer to those who can. This lesson underscores that a mufti is not a passive transmitter of texts but an active jurist who weighs custom and public interest within the bounds of Shariah.

Key Terms
العرف الحادث (al-urf al-hadith): The emerging or contemporary custom that has become prevalent among the people of a particular time, which may necessitate a shift in fatwa from the apparent ruling of the madhhab.
المصلحة (al-maslahah): Public interest or benefit that is recognized by Shariah and reason; the mufti may choose the ruling that serves maslahah for the people, even if it is not the most apparent position in the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a mufti do when there are multiple narrations (aqwal) without a clear preference?
A) He should abstain from issuing a fatwa until he finds a definitive preference.
B) He acts based on consideration of change of custom, conditions of people, what is more beneficial, and what has become the practice, then chooses accordingly.
C) He should always adopt the view of Abu Hanifah.
D) He must follow the majority of later scholars blindly.

Key Takeaway
Custom can override the apparent madhhab ruling when it serves public interest and does not oppose Shariah; the mufti must be a discerning jurist, not a passive transmitter.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Custom and Its Limits in Fatwa
Rules for mufti regarding valid and corrupt custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that valid custom is a source for fatwa and identify corrupt custom that must be rejected.

Core Idea
Custom is authoritative unless it opposes Shariah; the mufti must discern valid from corrupt custom.

This passage from the Hanafi iftaa tradition explains how a mufti should handle custom (urf) when issuing fatwas. It distinguishes between valid custom (urf sahih), which is a legitimate basis for rulings, and corrupt custom (urf fasid), which violates Shariah and must be rejected. The text also provides practical examples and emphasizes the mufti's need for ijtihad and awareness of changing circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish valid from corrupt custom
Identify the criteria from the text: valid custom does not make halal what is haram or vice versa; corrupt custom does.
The examples given: istisna' payment custom (valid) vs riba transactions (corrupt).
2
Apply the qawida 'لا يعمل بالعرف إذا خالف الشريعة'
Understand that this maxim overrides custom when it contradicts a clear nass or ijma. Custom is secondary.
This is a wusta (intermediate) maxim governing all fiqh chapters.
3
Analyze the fasting example (meat between teeth)
See how custom and personal disposition affect the fatwa: Abu Yusuf's view considers whether the person naturally abhors such eating; Zafar's view does not.
The mufti must consider the person's custom and habit to determine if kaffarah applies.
4
Recognize the mufti's duty to consult when unsure
The passage states the world is never devoid of one who can truly distinguish valid from corrupt custom. If a mufti cannot, he must refer to such a scholar.
This ensures the fatwa is based on sound understanding of custom.

Source Text

العرف الحادث
أهلها، معتمداً على عدم قبول شهادتهم عليه ، حتى قلت : ينبغي الفتوى على قولهما، لا سيما والأحكام تختلف باختلاف الأيام "
وقال في "فتح القدير " في باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة من كتاب الصوم عند قول "الهداية " : " ولو أكل لحما بين أسنانه لم يفطر، وإن كان كثيراً يفطر، وقال زفر : يفطر في الوجهين " . اهـ . ما نصه : " والتحقيق أن المفتى في الوقائع لا بد له من ضرب اجتهاد ومعرفة بأحوال الناس، وقد عرف أن الكفارة تفتقر إلى كمال الجناية، فينظر إلى صاحب الواقعة : إن كان ممن يعاف طبعه ذلك ، أخذ بقول أبي يوسف، وإن كان ممن لا أثر لذلك عنده أخذ بقول زفر ) . اهـ . وفى تصحيح العلامة قاسم " : "فإن قلت : قد يحكون أقوالا من غير ترجیح ، وقد يختلفون في التصحيح؟ قلت : يعمل بمثل ما عملوا من اعتبار تغيّر العرف ، وأحوال الناس، وما هو الأرفق بالناس، وما ظهر عليه التعامل، وما قوى وجهه، ولا يخلو الوجود من تمييز هذا (۲) حقيقة لا ظنّا بنفسه (۳) ، ويرجع من لم يميز إلى من يميز البراءة ذمته " . اهـ .
(۱) قوله : "أخذ بقول زفر وهذا قريب مما قاله أبو نصر محمد بن سلام من كبار أئمة الحنفية، وبعض أئمة المالكية في إفطار السلطان في رمضان : أنه يفتى بصيام شهرين؛ لأن المقصود من الكفارة الانزجار ، ويسهل عليه إفطار شهر وإعتاق رقبة فلا يحصل الزجر . رسائل ابن عابدین : ۱۳۰۲
(۲) قوله : ولا يخلو الوجود من تمييز هذا هكذا في النسخ الثلاثة الموجودة عندنا ، ولا يخفى ما فيه من خلل. وقد نقل العلامة الحصكفى هذه العبارة في الدر المختار ونصه : " ولا يخلو الوجود عمن يميز هذا حقيقة لا ظنّا، وعلى من لم يميز أن يرجع لمن يميز ؛ لبراءة ذمته " . (۷۸/۱)
(۳) قوله : "حقيقة لاظنا بنفسه " كذا في النسخ الثلاثة الموجودة عندنا . ولعل لفظ بنفسه" من زيادة النساخ ، فقد نقلت عبارة العلامة قاسم هذه في آخر مقدمة الدر
المختار ولا يوجد فيه هذا اللفظ ولا يصح من وجه فالصواب إسقاطه. وفيها : ثم إنما جزم بعدم خلو الزمان ممن يقدر على هذا التمييز ؛ أخذا مما رواه البخارى من قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «لا يزال طائفة من أمتى ظاهرين على الحق حتى يأتي أمر الله وفي رواية حتى تأتي الساعة». (۷۸/۱)

العرف إذا خالف الشريعة
مطلب
لا يعمل بالعرف إذا خالف الشريعة
فهذا كله صريح فيما قلنا من العمل بالعرف ما لم يخالف الشريعة (1)
كا المكس والربا ونحو ذلك .

(۱) قوله : " ما لم يخالف الشريعة اعلم أن العرف بالنظر إلى كونه مشروعا أو غیر مشروع ينقسم إلى صحيح وفاسد. فالعرف الصحيح : هو ما تعارفه الناس دون أن يحرم حلالا أو يحل حراما ، كتعارفهم تقديم بعض الأجرة في عقد الاستصناع، وتعارفهم أن الزوجة لا تنتقل إلى بيت زوجها إلا بعد قبض جزء من المهر، وأن المهر قسمان مقدم ومؤخر، وأن ما يقدمه الخاطب أثناء الخطبة يعتبر هدية وليس جزء من المهر
والعرف الفاسد : هو ما تعارفه الناس ولكنه يحل حرامًا أو يحرم حلالا ، كتعارفهم المعاملات المشتملة على الربا والقمار واختلاط النساء بالرجال في الحفلات والأندية العامة، وترك الصلاة في الاحتفالات العامة والضيافة من أهل الميت وإنارة السراج على القبور وغير ذلك من البدعات والمنكرات . كذا في أصول الفقه للزحيلي بتغيير يسير . (۸۳۰/٢)..
The new custom (al-urf al-hadith). Its people, relying on the non-acceptance of their testimony against him, until I said: Fatwa should be given according to their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) statement, especially since rulings differ with the difference of times. And he said in Fath al-Qadir, in the chapter on what necessitates qada and kaffarah from the book of fasting, at the statement of al-Hidayah: 'If someone ate meat between his teeth, he does not break his fast, but if it was a lot he breaks his fast, and Zafar said: he breaks his fast in both cases.' The text: 'The investigation is that the mufti in incidents must have a certain amount of ijtihad and knowledge of the conditions of people, and it is known that kaffarah requires complete (intentional) violation. So he considers the person involved: if he is one whose nature would abhor that, he takes the statement of Abu Yusuf; if he is one for whom there is no effect (disgust) in that, he takes the statement of Zafar.' And in the correction of Allamah Qasim: 'If you say: They sometimes report statements without preference (tarjih), and they differ in correction? I say: He acts according to what they acted upon, i.e., considering the change of custom, the conditions of people, what is more convenient for people, what has become customary in transactions, and what is stronger in its reasoning. And the world is not devoid of one who distinguishes this truly, not by guesswork (by himself). And whoever does not distinguish should refer to one who distinguishes for the acquittal of his responsibility.' (Footnote 1: His statement: 'He takes the statement of Zafar' is close to what Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn Salam, a major Hanafi imam, and some Maliki imams said regarding break-fast of a sultan in Ramadan: that he gives fatwa of fasting two months, because the purpose of kaffarah is deterrence, and it is easy for him to fast a month or free a slave, so deterrence is not achieved. (Rasa'il Ibn Abidin 1:1302)) (Footnote 2: His statement: 'The world is not devoid of distinguishing this' is as in the three copies we have, and it is not hidden what deficiency is in it. Allamah al-Haskafi transmitted this phrase in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its text: 'The world is not devoid of one who distinguishes this truly, not by guesswork, and whoever does not distinguish must refer to one who distinguishes for the acquittal of his responsibility.' (1:78)) (Footnote 3: His statement: 'truly, not by guesswork (by himself)' is as in the three copies we have. Perhaps the phrase 'by himself' is an addition by the scribes, for the statement of Allamah Qasim is quoted at the end of the introduction of al-Durr al-Mukhtar and that phrase is not found there, and it is not correct in any way, so the correct is to drop it. And in it: Then he asserted that time is not devoid of someone capable of this distinction, based on what Bukhari narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'A group of my ummah will continue to be manifest upon the truth until Allah's command comes' and in another narration 'until the Hour comes.' (1:78))

Custom when it opposes Shariah. Section: Custom is not acted upon when it opposes Shariah. So all this is explicit in what we said: acting upon custom as long as it does not oppose Shariah, like bribery and usury and the like. (Footnote 1: His statement: 'as long as it does not oppose Shariah.' Know that custom, with regard to being legislated or not, is divided into valid and corrupt. Valid custom is what people have mutually adopted without making lawful what is unlawful or unlawful what is lawful, such as their custom of paying part of the wage in istisna' contracts, and their custom that a wife does not move to her husband's house until after receiving part of the mahr, and that the mahr is divided into prompt and deferred, and that what the groom presents during the engagement is considered a gift and not part of the mahr. Corrupt custom is what people have mutually adopted but it makes lawful what is unlawful or makes unlawful what is lawful, such as their customs involving transactions containing usury and gambling, mixing of women and men in gatherings and public clubs, abandoning prayer in public celebrations and hospitality from the deceased's family, lighting lamps on graves, and other innovations and reprehensible acts. (Quoted from Usul al-Fiqh by al-Zuhayli with slight modification. 2:830))
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage discusses the role of custom (urf) in fatwa and its limits. It begins by referring to a new custom (al-urf al-hadith) and the need to adopt the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) because rulings change with changing times. It then cites a detailed example from Fath al-Qadir about eating meat between teeth in fasting: Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions hold that if it is a lot, it breaks the fast; Zafar holds it always breaks the fast. The author, Ibn al-Humam, says the mufti must have ijtihad and knowledge of people's conditions. For kaffarah, which requires complete intentional violation, the mufti should consider the person: if he would naturally abhor such eating, then he takes Abu Yusuf's view (no kaffarah, since it is not a complete violation in his habit); if he does not mind, then Zafar's view (kaffarah). This shows that custom and personal disposition affect the fatwa.

Then Allamah Qasim's correction addresses the problem when jurists report multiple views without preference and differ in correction. He says the mufti should follow their method: consider changing custom, people's conditions, what is more convenient, what people practice, and what is stronger in reasoning. The world is never devoid of someone capable of this true distinction (not guesswork), and the non-distinguishing mufti must consult such a scholar to acquit his responsibility.

The second part explicitly states the rule: Custom is not acted upon if it opposes Shariah. It then defines valid custom (urf sahih) and corrupt custom (urf fasid) with examples. Valid custom does not make the lawful unlawful or vice versa; corrupt custom does, like riba, gambling, gender mixing, etc.

Authority weight: This is from the later commentary tradition (Ibn Abidin, al-Haskafi), not from Zahir al-Riwayah. The definitions of urf sahih and fasid are standard Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
العرف الحادث (al-urf al-hadith): A custom that emerges newly; the mufti must consider it if it is valid and does not oppose Shariah.
العرف الصحيح (al-urf al-sahih): A custom that does not make halal what is haram or haram what is halal; it is acted upon.
العرف الفاسد (al-urf al-fasid): A custom that makes halal what is haram or haram what is halal; it is rejected.
المفتى (al-mufti): The one who issues fatwas; must have ijtihad to distinguish applicable rulings based on custom and conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of valid custom (urf sahih)?
A) Paying part of the wage in istisna' contracts
B) Mixing of men and women in public gatherings
C) Transactions involving riba
D) Lighting lamps on graves

Key Takeaway
A mufti must accept valid custom as a source for fatwa if it does not oppose Shariah, and reject corrupt custom that violates it.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

The Mufti's Ijtihad and Awareness of People's Circumstances
Arguing that a mufti must exercise ijtihad and know people's circumstances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two essential competencies of a mufti (ijtihad and knowledge of people's conditions) and apply the principle that fatwas may be withheld to prevent oppression.

Core Idea
A mufti must exercise ijtihad and understand people's realities; blind textualism causes injustice.

The role of the mufti is not merely to transmit rulings but to apply them wisely in changing contexts. This lesson from Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti establishes that the mufti must possess a level of ijtihad and be well-versed in the conditions, customs, and deceptions of people. Without this, fatwas may lead to the loss of rights and even enable oppression.

Guided Path
1
State the core requirement
Identify that the mufti must have a level of ijtihad (ḍarb ijtihād). This means he must be able to go beyond mere transmission and reason with the sources.
Not absolute ijtihad, but sufficient competence.
2
Explain the condemnation of rigidity
Rigidity on apparent texts (jumūd ʿalā al-Ẓāhir) leads to wasting rights (taḍyīʿ al-ḥuqūq). The mufti must consider custom and circumstances.
This is the central maxim of the passage.
3
Describe the need to know people's circumstances
The mufti must know people's conditions, customs, and potential deceit. The text cites Ibn Qayyim: 'Whoever is ignorant of his time's people is ignorant.'
Includes understanding of 'urf and qara'in.
4
Examine the example of the landowner
If a landowner plants a lesser crop despite ability to plant a superior one, he must pay kharaj of the superior crop. But this ruling is known and not given as a fatwa.
Publicizing it would allow oppressors to falsely claim high-value crops and demand excessive kharaj.
5
Derive the principle of withholding fatwas
Some correct rulings are deliberately withheld to prevent greater harm (dar' al-mafasid). This is part of the mufti's ijtihad and knowledge of people.
Other examples given: disciplining by taking money, Hanafi following Shafi'i.

Explanation of 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti
190: The mufti must have a level of ijtihad. Topic: The mufti must have a level of ijtihad, because his rigidity on the apparent (text) may lead to wasting rights. (1) So the mufti, the judge, and even the mujtahid must know the conditions of people. They said: 'Whoever is ignorant of his time's people is ignorant.' We have previously mentioned that they said: Fatwa is given according to Abu Yusuf's opinion regarding judicial matters because he experienced incidents and knew people's conditions. In al-Bahr, from Manaqib al-Imam Muhammad by al-Kardari: 'Muhammad used to go to the dyers, ask about their transactions and what they conducted among themselves.' End quote. They said: 'If the landowner plants his land with something inferior while he is capable of [planting] a superior [crop], he must pay the kharaj of the superior [crop].' They said: 'This is known but not given as a fatwa, so that oppressors do not dare to take people's wealth.'
(1) His statement: 'Knowledge of people's conditions' – Ibn Qayyim, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'The fifth [principle]: knowledge of people. This is a great foundation needed by the mufti and the judge. If he is not a jurist in this, [though] he is a jurist in commanding and forbidding, then he applies one to the other, otherwise he will cause more corruption than reform. For if he is not a jurist in [understanding] people, the oppressor appears to him as the oppressed and vice versa, the rightful as the wrongdoer and vice versa, and deception, trickery, and fraud are prevalent to him, and the heretic appears as the truthful one, the liar as the truthful one, and every wrongdoer dons a garment of falsehood beneath which is sin, lying, and immorality – due to his ignorance of people, their conditions, customs, and conventions, he cannot distinguish one from another. Rather, he must be a jurist in knowing people's deceit, trickery, fraud, customs, and conventions, for fatwa changes with changes in time, place, customs, and conditions, and all of that is part of the religion of Allah as previously explained. And with Allah is success.'
I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in 4/204

191: Explanation of 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: The mufti must have a level of ijtihad, so that oppressors do not dare to take people's wealth. He said in al-'Inayah: 'It was objected: How is concealment permissible? If they took it, it would be in its proper place because it is obligatory.' The answer: If we gave a fatwa on that, every oppressor would claim regarding a land that is not suitable for that, that it was previously planted with saffron, for example, and then he takes its kharaj, and that is oppression and transgression.' End quote.
Similarly, in Fath al-Qadir: 'They said: No fatwa is given on this (1) because it leads to oppressors dominating the wealth of Muslims, as every oppressor would claim that the land is suitable for planting saffron and the like, and its treatment is difficult.' End quote.
Thus it has become clear to you that the mufti's or judge's rigidity on the apparent transmitted [text], while ignoring custom and clear circumstantial evidence, and ignorance of people's conditions, necessarily leads to wasting many rights and oppressing many creatures.
(2)
(1) His statement: 'No fatwa is given on this' – I say: Among these issues is what is in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its gloss: Whoever persists in abandoning the congregation out of laziness, he is disciplined by taking money from him for a period; this is known and concealed, because oppressors are hunters for money – whenever it falls into their hands, they do not return it, and perhaps they invent a sin for a person that he did not commit to get his wealth (1/556). Also: It is permissible for a Hanafi to follow al-Shafi'i by the judgment of a judge-arbitrator or even the fatwa of a just [scholar] as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar (3/346-348); rather, the arbitration of a follower of another madhhab is valid in all matters of ijtihad as also in al-Durr (5/340); but this is known and not given as a fatwa so that common people do not dare to destroy the madhhab. Similarly, they said: One who undertakes distributing the [land] tax justly is rewarded even if taking is oppression, but this is known and not made known so that oppressors do not dare to increase beyond the due amount. See the previous source 2/336.
(2) His statement: 'oppressing many creatures' – For we see a man coming as a seeker of a fatwa regarding a legal ruling, while his intention is to harm others through that. If we issue a fatwa to him about what he asked, we would have participated in the sin because he did not achieve his intended goal except by our means. For example: if he comes asking about a sister of his who is in the custody of her mother, and the custody period has ended, and he wants to take her from
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti establishes a foundational rasm rule for muftis: the mufti must possess a degree of ijtihad and cannot blindly adhere to the literal text (zahir) of transmitted rulings. The author opens by stating the necessity of ijtihad, warning that rigidity leads to injustice. He then expands: the mufti, judge, and even mujtahid must understand people's circumstances. He cites the maxim 'Whoever is ignorant of his time's people is ignorant,' and notes that in judicial matters, fatwa is given according to Abu Yusuf because he experienced real cases and knew people. An example from Imam Muhammad shows his engagement with merchants to understand their practices. The text then presents a case: if a landowner plants a lesser crop despite being capable of a superior one, he must pay the kharaj of the superior crop. However, the scholars ruled that this is known but not publicly issued as a fatwa, to prevent oppressors from exploiting it to seize land and claim high kharaj. The author supports this with quotes from al-'Inayah and Fath al-Qadir, explaining that such a fatwa would allow every oppressor to claim his land previously grew saffron and demand excessive taxes. The footnote from Ibn Qayyim in I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in underscores the importance of knowing people, deception, and customs, stating that fatwa changes with time, place, and circumstances. The second footnote provides further examples where fatwas are withheld to prevent harm: disciplining a person abandoning jama'ah by taking money (to avoid oppression), and the permissibility of a Hanafi following Shafi'i in certain matters (to protect the madhhab from commoners dismantling it). The lesson concludes that rigidity and ignorance lead to widespread injustice, illustrated by a case where a man seeks a fatwa to harm his sister in custody.

Key Terms
ضرب اجتهاد (ḍarb ijtihād): A level of ijtihad; the mufti must have the ability to exert legal reasoning beyond mere transmission.
جمود على الظاهر (jumūd ʿalā al-Ẓāhir): Rigid adherence to the literal or apparent meaning of transmitted texts without considering context or consequences.
تضييع الحقوق (taḍyīʿ al-ḥuqūq): Wasting or losing rights; the consequence of improper fatwa issuance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the crop type is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must exercise ijtihad and know people's conditions; blindly following texts can cause injustice, and some correct rulings must be withheld to prevent greater harm.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Custom Can Specify, Not Abandon a Text
Rasm al-mufti rule on custom specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rasm al-mufti rule that general custom can specify (takhsis) a textual ruling but cannot completely abandon (tark) it, and distinguish general custom from specific custom.

Core Idea
Custom specifies a text, it does not nullify it. General custom is a proof; specific custom is not.

This passage from al-Dhakhira lays down a foundational principle for muftis: custom (ta'amul) can narrow the scope of a textual ruling but cannot erase it entirely. The distinction between specification (takhsis) and abandonment (tark) is crucial, as is the divide between general custom (urf 'amm) and local custom (urf khas). Understanding this rule prevents muftis from invalidating texts when they encounter widespread practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the textual ruling at stake
Find the explicit text (nass) that the custom interacts with. In the example, the text prohibits 'selling what one does not possess' — a hadith. The custom concerns istisna' (manufacturing contract) which involves selling what one does not yet own.
2
Distinguish takhsis from tark
Check whether the custom carves out an exception within the text's scope (takhsis) or nullifies it entirely (tark). If the text still applies to cases outside the custom, it's specification; if the custom makes the text completely inoperative, it's abandonment.
Abandonment is impermissible; specification is allowed only for general custom.
3
Assess the custom's scope
Determine if the custom is universal (fixed in all towns, like istisna') or local (only in one town, like Balkh's weaver custom). General custom can specify; specific custom cannot, because its limited spread creates doubt (shakk).
If the custom is widespread, it is considered a public interest; if local, it cannot override a text.
4
Apply the rule to the mufti's case
For a given scenario, check if the custom leads to takhsis or tark. If it leads to tark, reject it. If it leads to takhsis, accept only if the custom is general. For local custom, it only governs among its people in matters without a text.
This step requires identifying the type of custom and the nature of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجوز تخصيص النص بالتعامل لا تركه
مطلب
التعامل العام يجوز به تخصيص النص لا تركه
قال في " الذخيرة " في الفصل الثامن من الإجارات في مسألة "ما لو دفع إلى حائك غزلا لينسجه بالثلث :
و مشایخ بلخ كنصير بن يحيى ومحمد بن سلمة وغيرهما، كانوا يجيزون هذه الإجارة في الثياب ؛ لتعامل أهل بلدهم في الثياب، والتعامل حجة يترك به القياس، ويخص به الأثر (۱) . وتجويز هذه الإجارة في الثياب للتعامل، بمعنى تخصيص النص الذي ورد في قفيز الطحان ؛ لأن النص ورد في قفيز الطحان لا في الحائك، إلا أن الحائك نظيره فيكون واردا فيه دلالة، فمتى تركنا العمل بدلالة هذا النص في الحائك ، وعملنا بالنص في قـفـيـز الطحان كان تخصيصاً لا تركاً أصلا، وتخصيص النص بالتعامل جائز . ألا ترى أنا جوزنا الاستصناع للتعامل والاستصناع بيع ما ليس عنده وإنه منهى عنه، وتجويز الاستصناع بالتعامل تخصيص منا للنص الذي ورد في النهي عن بيع ما ليس عند الإنسان، لا ترك للنص أصلا ؛ لأنا عملنا بالنص فى غير الاستصناع . قالوا : وهذا بخلاف ما لو تعامل أهل بلدة قفيز الطحان ؛ فإنه لا يجوز، ولا تكون معاملتهم معتبرة ؛ لأنا لو اعتبرنا معاملتهم كان تركا للنص أصلا، وبالتعامل لا يجوز ترك النص أصلا، وإنما يجوز تخصيصه، ولكن مشايخنا لم يجوزوا هذا التخصيص (۱) ؛ لأن ذلك تعامل أهل بلدة واحدة، وتعامل أهل بلدة واحدة لا يخص الأثر ؛ لأن تعامل أهل بلدة إن اقتضى أن يجوز التخصيص، فترك التعامل من أهل بلدة أخرى يمنع التخصيص، فلا يثبت التخصيص بالشك، بخلاف التعامل في الاستصناع، فإنه وجد في البلاد كلها . انتهى كلام "الذخيرة ". 
مطلب
في اعتبار العرف العام والخاص
والحاصل (۲): أن العرف العام لا يعتبر إذا لزم منه ترك المنصوص، وإنما يعتبر إذا لزم منه تخصيص النص. والعرف الخاص لا يعتبر في الموضعين، وإنما يعتبر في حق أهله فقط إذا لم يلزم منه ترك النص ولا تخصيصه، وإنما يعتبر في حق أهله فقط إذا لم يلزم منه ترك النص ولا تخصيصه، وإنما يعتبر في حق أهله فقط إذا لم يلزم منه ترك النص ولا تخصيصه
It is permissible to specify the text by custom, not to abandon it. Section: General custom may be used to specify the text, not to abandon it. He said in al-Dhakhira in the eighth chapter of rentals concerning the issue: 'If someone gives yarn to a weaver to weave it for a third [of the cloth]... And the scholars of Balkh, like Nasir ibn Yahya and Muhammad ibn Salama and others, used to permit this rental for cloth due to the custom of the people of their town in cloth. Custom is a proof by which analogy is abandoned and the transmitted text is specified. And permitting this rental for cloth due to custom means specifying the text that came concerning the qafiz of the miller. For the text came concerning the qafiz of the miller, not concerning the weaver, except that the weaver is analogous to it, so it applies to it by implication. When we abandon acting upon the implication of this text regarding the weaver and act upon the text regarding the qafiz of the miller, this is specification, not abandonment at all. And specifying the text by custom is permissible. Do you not see that we permitted istisna' due to custom, while istisna' is selling what one does not possess, and it is prohibited? Permitting istisna' due to custom is our specification of the text that came prohibiting selling what one does not possess, not an abandonment of the text at all, for we act upon the text in cases other than istisna'. They said: This is unlike if the people of a town transact using the qafiz of the miller; then it is not permissible, and their transaction is not considered, for if we considered their transaction it would be an abandonment of the text entirely, and by custom it is not permissible to abandon the text entirely, only to specify it. But our scholars did not permit this specification because that is the custom of one town only, and the custom of one town does not specify the transmitted text. For if the custom of one town necessitates that specification be permissible, then the absence of custom in another town prevents the specification, so specification is not established due to doubt, unlike the custom in istisna', which existed in all towns.' End of the quote from al-Dhakhira. Section: On considering general and specific custom. The conclusion: General custom is not considered if it necessitates abandoning what is textually stated; rather it is considered only if it necessitates specification of the text. Specific custom is not considered in either case; it is considered only among its people when it necessitates neither abandoning the text nor specifying it.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule concerning the legal weight of customary practice ('urf or ta'amul) in the Hanafi school. The core principle is that custom can specify (takhsis) a textual ruling but cannot completely abandon (tark) it. The author opens by citing al-Dhakhira, which discusses the permissibility of a weaver's rental contract based on the custom of the people of Balkh. The scholars of Balkh allowed this contract even though it is analogous to a prohibited transaction (the qafiz of the miller), because custom serves as a proof that overrides analogy and can specify the text. The key distinction is between specification and abandonment: in the weaver case, the general prohibition (qafiz of the miller) is still applied to situations not covered by custom, so it is specification. In contrast, if a single town adopts a practice directly contradicting the text (like using the miller's qafiz), that would be abandonment, which custom cannot justify. However, the scholars of Balkh argued that the weaver case is specification because it only applies to cloth, not to other analogies. The author then notes that the later Hanafi scholars (mashayikhuna) did not allow specification by the custom of a single town, because such specific custom cannot outweigh the transmitted text. General custom (like istisna' which spread across all lands) is different—it can specify. The passage concludes with a summary of the conditions for considering custom: general custom is effective only if it leads to specification, not abandonment; specific custom is not effective for either, except among its own people in matters that do not conflict with any textual ruling. This is a critical refinement for muftis: they must distinguish between custom that merely narrows a text and custom that would nullify it entirely.

Key Terms
التعامل (al-ta'amul): Customary practice or transaction among people, considered as a legal proof.
التخصيص (al-takhsis): Specifying a general text to exclude certain cases, narrowing its scope without nullifying it.
الترك (al-tark): Abandoning the application of a text entirely, which is impermissible by custom.
العرف العام (al-urf al-'amm): Custom that is widespread across all regions and not limited to a specific locality.
العرف الخاص (al-urf al-khas): Custom limited to a specific town or group, not universally practiced.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rasm al-mufti discussed, what is the effect of a general, widespread custom (urf 'amm) on a textual ruling?
A) It can completely abandon the text and replace it.
B) It can specify the text (takhsis), narrowing its scope, but cannot abandon it.
C) It has no effect at all on any textual ruling.
D) It can only specify the text if there is consensus among all jurists.

Key Takeaway
General custom can specify a text but not abandon it; specific custom cannot specify texts and only applies among its people in matters without a textual ruling.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
