Conditions for Considering General and Specific Custom
Explains when general and specific custom are legally considered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida on general and specific custom to determine when custom overrides apparent school rulings.

Core Idea
General custom specifies nass but does not abandon it; specific custom only binds its own people.

Custom (urf) plays a vital role in Hanafi jurisprudence, especially in interpreting contracts, oaths, and other legal expressions. But not every custom carries the same weight. This lesson clarifies the conditions under which general and specific custom are considered, and when they yield to explicit textual rulings. Mastering this distinction is essential for a mufti tasked with applying fiqh to real-world scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of custom
Distinguish between general custom (urf aam) and specific custom (urf khaas) as defined in the passage. General custom is widespread; specific custom is confined to a particular group or locality.
General custom can affect textual interpretation, but specific custom is more restricted.
2
Apply the rule for general custom
General custom is only considered if it entails specifying (takhsis) a text, not if it would lead to abandoning (tark) the text entirely. Determine whether the custom in question specifies a zanni text or contradicts a qat'i text.
Abandoning a nass is never permitted; specification of a zanni nass is allowed.
3
Apply the rule for specific custom
Specific custom is not considered for specifying or abandoning any nass. It is only considered for its own people (ahl) when it does not conflict with a nass. However, it can override the apparent school opinion (zahir al-riwaya).
Specific custom may change the fatwa for that community even if it contradicts the standard Hanafi view.
4
Analyze examples from the passage
Review the examples of oaths and contracts. In oaths, the speaker's local custom defines the intended meaning, even if jurists typically define the phrase differently. In contracts, the usage of the people determines the terms.
The principle: 'Absolute speech among people is interpreted according to what is customary.'
5
Identify exceptions and limitations
Both general and specific custom are invalid if they require abandoning a definitive nass. Specific custom also cannot specify a nass. Recognize that custom operates within the bounds of revelation.
The istithna'at clarify the boundaries: custom cannot override qat'i proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل: أن العرف العام لا يعتبر إذا لزم منه ترك المنصوص، وإنما يعتبر إذا لزم منه تخصيص النص. والعرف الخاص لا يعتبر في الموضعين، وإنما يعتبر في حق أهله فقط إذا لم يلزم منه ترك النص ولا تخصيصه، وإن خالف ظاهر الرواية. وذلك كما فى الألفاظ المتعارفة في الأيمان، والعادة الجارية في العقود من بيع وإجارة ونحوها، فتجرى تلك الألفاظ والعقود في كل بلدة على عادة أهلها، ويراد منها ذلك المعتاد بينهم، ويعاملون دون غيرهم بما يقتضيه ذلك من صحة وفساد، وتحريم وتحليل، وغير ذلك، وإن صرح الفقهاء بأن مقتضاه خلاف ما اقتضاه العرف؛ لأن المتكلّم إنما يتكلّم على عرفه وعادته، ويقصد ذلك بكلامه دون ما أراده الفقهاء، وإنما يعامل كل أحد بما أراده. والألفاظ العرفية حقائق اصطلاحية يصير بها المعنى الأصلي كالمجاز اللغوى. قال في جامع الفصولين: "مطلق الكلام فيما بين الناس ينصرف إلى المتعارف". وفي فتاوى العلامة قاسم: التحقيق أن لفظ الواقف، والموصى، والحالف، والناذر، وكل عاقد - يحمل على عادته في خطابه ولغته التي يتكلّم بها، وافقت لغة العرب ولغة الشارع أولا.
The summary: General custom is not considered if it entails leaving the text (nass); rather it is considered only if it entails specifying (takhsis) the text. Specific custom is not considered in either of the two situations; rather it is considered only with respect to its own people if it does not entail leaving the text nor specifying it, even if it contradicts the apparent ruling (zahir al-riwaya). That is like customary expressions in oaths, and common practice in contracts like sale, lease and the like. These expressions and contracts are conducted in each town according to the custom of its people, and what is meant thereby is that customary practice among them, and they are treated—unlike others—according to what that custom requires in terms of validity and invalidity, prohibition and permissibility, etc., even if the jurists have stated that the apparent ruling (mujtaha) of such expressions is contrary to what the custom requires; because a speaker speaks according to his custom and habit and intends that by his speech, not what the jurists intended, and each person is treated according to what he intended. Customary expressions are conventional realities that render the original meaning like a linguistic metaphor. It is stated in Jami' al-Fusulayn: 'Absolute speech among people is interpreted according to what is customary.' And in Fatawa al-Allamah Qasim: 'The verification is that the words of a waqif, a testator, a swearer, a person vowing, and every contracting party are interpreted according to his custom in his speech and his language with which he speaks, whether it agrees with the language of the Arabs and the language of the Lawgiver or not.'
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti defines the criteria for considering custom (urf) in Hanafi fiqh. The author first distinguishes between general custom (urf aam) and specific custom (urf khaas). General custom is only considered if it specifies (takhsis) a textual ruling, not if it completely abandons it. Specific custom is never considered to abrogate or specify a textual ruling; however, it is considered for the people who practice it in matters that do not conflict with a textual ruling, even if it contradicts the apparent transmitted opinion (zahir al-riwaya) of the school. This is because custom determines the intended meaning of spoken words in oaths, contracts, etc. People are judged according to their own customary usage, not according to jurists' technical definitions. The author cites Jami' al-Fusulayn and Fatawa al-Allamah Qasim to support that speech is interpreted based on the speaker's custom and language, regardless of classical Arabic or Shari'ah terminology. Thus, in Hanafi fatwa, custom plays a crucial role in interpreting legal statements and determining their effects, provided it does not negate an explicit primary text (nass qat'i).

Key Terms
العرف العام (al-'urf al-'aam): A custom prevalent among all people or in most regions, not confined to a specific locality.
العرف الخاص (al-'urf al-khaass): A custom specific to a particular town, group, or profession.
تخصيص النص (takhsis al-nass): Limiting the general applicability of a textual ruling based on custom or other evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is specific custom (urf khaas) considered in Hanafi fiqh?
A) It is always considered because custom is authoritative.
B) It is considered only for its own people if it does not entail abandoning or specifying a textual ruling (nass).
C) It is never considered because it contradicts the apparent school ruling.
D) It is considered only if it is general among all people.

Key Takeaway
Custom is authoritative but limited: general custom can specify a text, specific custom binds its own people, and neither can override a definitive nass.

Considering Specific Custom Despite Zahir al-Riwayah: The Rule of Urf Khas
Rasm al-mufti rule on urf khas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rasm al-mufti rule that specific custom (urf khas) is considered in fatwa even when it contradicts the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), by distinguishing between opposition to shari text and madhhab text.

Core Idea
Custom overrides madhhab texts when it reflects the speaker's intention, but not shari texts.

In the Hanafi school, custom (urf) is a recognized source of law, but its authority relative to the transmitted texts of the madhhab (zahir al-riwayah) is not absolute. This lesson examines a critical rasm al-mufti principle: specific custom (urf khas) is given weight in fatwa even when it contradicts the apparent ruling from the founding imams, provided it does not oppose a definitive text from the Quran or Sunnah. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for a mufti who must issue rulings that are both faithful to the madhhab and relevant to the people's actual practices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Recall the maxim: 'Unrestricted speech is directed to the customary.' This foundational principle means that when people speak, their words are interpreted according to the custom of their community, not merely according to dictionary definitions.
This principle is quoted from Jami' al-Fusulayn in the passage.
2
Distinguish between two types of opposition
The passage clarifies that the earlier rule from al-Ashbah (that specific custom is not considered) applies only when custom opposes a clear shari text from Quran or Sunnah. When custom opposes a madhhab text (the transmitted ruling of the imams), it is considered.
This distinction is critical for the mufti's decision-making.
3
Analyze the supporting evidence
Study the evidence cited: the statement from Jami' al-Fusulayn, the fatwa of Allamah Qasim, and the incident of Ibn Umar with the man from Banu Ribah. Each shows that custom determines the intended meaning of speech.
The Ibn Umar incident shows that even a vow's object is interpreted by local custom.
4
Apply the rule to a real scenario
Consider a hypothetical: In a certain region, the word 'milk' is customarily used for fermented milk, but the madhhab text defines milk as fresh milk. When someone vows not to drink milk, the fatwa should follow the local custom because the speaker intended what is customary in his speech.
The mufta bihi is the custom-based ruling.
5
Recognize the limit of the rule
Note that this rule does not apply when custom contradicts a definitive nass from the Quran or mutawatir sunnah. In such cases, the nass prevails. This prevents the rule from undermining fundamental shar'iah commands.
The footnote from Nashr al-Urf reinforces this limit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدم اعتبار العرف الخاص خالف ظاهر الرواية (1) . وذلك كما فى الألفاظ المتعارفة في الأيمان، والعادة الجارية في العقود من بيع وإجارة ونحوها، فتجرى تلك الألفاظ والعقود في كل بلدة على عادة أهلها، ويراد منها ذلك المعتاد بينهم، ويعاملون دون غيرهم بما يقتضيه ذلك من صحة وفساد، وتحريم وتحليل، وغير ذلك، وإن صرح الفقهاء بأن مقتضاه خلاف ما اقتضاه العرف؛ لأن المتكلّم إنما يتكلّم على عرفه وعادته، ويقصد ذلك بكلامه دون ما أراده الفقهاء، وإنما يعامل كل أحد بما أراده. والألفاظ العرفية حقائق اصطلاحية يصير بها المعنى الأصلي كالمجاز اللغوى، قال في جامع الفصولين " : "مطلق الكلام فيما بين الناس ينصرف إلى المتعارف" . اهـ . وفي فتاوى العلامة قاسم : التحقيق أن لفظ الواقف، والموصى، (۲).. ،والحالف، والناذر ، وكل عاقد - يحمل على عادته في خطابه ولغته التي يتكلّم بها، وافقت لغة العرب ولغة الشارع أولا (۲) . اهـ . ثم اعلم أنى لم أر من تكلّم على هذه المسألة بما يشفى العليل، وكشفها يحتاج إلى زيادة تطويل ؛ لأن الكلام عليها يطول ؛ لاحتياجه إلى ذكر فروع وأصول ، وأجوبة عما عسى يقال، وتوضيح ما بنى على هذا المقال (1) قوله : " وإن خالف ظاهر الرواية " الحاصل : أن ما تقدم عن الأشباه من أن المذهب عدم اعتبار العرف الخاص، إنما هو فيما إذا عارض النص الشرعي، فلا يترك به القياس ولا يخص به الأثر، وأما إذا عارض النص المذهبي المنقول عن صاحب المذهب فهو معتبر، كما مشى عليه أصحاب المتون والشروح والفتاوى . كذا في نشر العرف" ، الملحق برسائل ابن عابدین : ۱۳۳/۲ . (2) قوله : "أولا" وفى شرح السير الكبير للسرخسي : الحاصل أنه يعتبر في كل موضع عرف أهل ذلك الموضع فيما يطلقون عليه من الاسم أصله : ما روى أن رجلا سأل ابن عمر رضى الله تعالى عنهما أن صاحباً لنا أوجب بدنة ، أفتجزيه البقرة ؟ فقال : ممن صاحبكم؟ فقال: من بنى رباح، فقال: ومتى اقتنت بنو رباح البقر ؟ إنما وهم صاحبكم الإبل" . من هامش رسائل ابن عابدین : ۱۳۳/۲)
Non-consideration of specific custom contrary to the apparent narration. This is like in the customary expressions in oaths, and the prevailing custom in contracts of sale, lease, and the like. So those expressions and contracts are applied in each town according to the custom of its people, and what is customary among them is intended, and they are dealt with accordingly in terms of validity and invalidity, prohibition and permissibility, etc., even if the jurists explicitly state that the requirement [of the apparent narration] is contrary to what custom requires; because the speaker speaks according to his custom and habit, and intends that by his speech, not what the jurists intended, and everyone is dealt with according to what he intended. Customary expressions are terminological realities by which the original meaning becomes like a linguistic metaphor. It is stated in Jami' al-Fusulayn: 'Unrestricted speech among people is directed to the customary.' End. And in the Fatawa of Allamah Qasim: 'The verification is that the utterance of the waqf founder, the testator, the one swearing, the one vowing, and every contractor is interpreted according to his custom in his speech and the language he speaks, whether it agrees with the language of the Arabs and the language of the Lawgiver or not.' End. Then know that I have not seen anyone discuss this issue in a way that quenches the thirst [of the seeker], and its clarification requires more lengthy treatment; because discussion on it is lengthy due to the need to mention branches and principles, and answers to what might be said, and clarification of what is built upon this statement. (1) His saying: 'Even if it contradicts the apparent narration' — the summary: What was mentioned earlier from al-Ashbah that the madhhab does not consider specific custom, is only when it opposes a textual [shar'i] evidence; then qiyas is not abandoned for it nor is the athar [text] specified by it. But when it opposes the madhhab text transmitted from the founder of the madhhab, it is considered, as followed by the authors of matn, commentaries, and fatwas. Thus in Nashr al-Urf. (2) His saying: 'or not' — In Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir for al-Sarakhsi: 'The summary is that in every place the custom of the people of that place is considered regarding what they apply as a name. Its basis is what is narrated that a man asked Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) that a companion of ours has vowed a badana, is a cow sufficient for him? He said: Where is your companion from? He said: From Banu Ribah. He said: When did Banu Ribah acquire cows? Your companion only intended camels.' From the margin of Rasa'il Ibn Abidin.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage discusses a rasm al-mufti rule: the consideration of specific custom (urf khas) in fatwa even when it contradicts the zahir al-riwayah (the apparent transmitted ruling from the founding imams). The author explains that custom is given weight in practical legal matters such as oaths, contracts, and utterances because people speak according to their own customs. The custom is considered a terminological reality that shifts the meaning from the original linguistic sense to the customary sense, similar to a metaphor. The author quotes Jami' al-Fusulayn and Allamah Qasim to support that the speech of individuals is interpreted according to their custom. The author then notes that the rule from al-Ashbah that custom is not considered only applies when custom opposes a textual evidence from the Quran or Sunnah; but when it opposes a madhhab text (i.e., a ruling from Abu Hanifa or his students), custom is considered because it reflects the intention of the speaker. The footnote from Nashr al-Urf clarifies this distinction. The example from Ibn Umar shows how custom determines the meaning of a vow in a specific locale. Thus, this is a refinement of the general rule about custom: it is not rejected altogether but is weighed against the source of the opposing text.

Key Terms
العرف الخاص (al-urfu al-khas): A custom that is specific to a particular locality, group, or context, as opposed to general custom that is widely known.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent transmitted rulings from the founders of the Hanafi madhhab (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) that are recorded in the well-known books of the school.
المجاز اللغوى (al-majaz al-lughawi): A linguistic metaphor where a word is used in a non-primary meaning due to a customary usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a specific custom (urf khas) considered in fatwa even if it contradicts the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah)?
A) Only when the custom is widely accepted in all Muslim lands.
B) When the custom opposes a text of the madhhab transmitted from the imams, but not when it opposes a definitive textual evidence from Quran or Sunnah.
C) Never, because the madhhab does not consider custom.
D) Only for oaths and vows, not for contracts.

Key Takeaway
Specific custom is considered in fatwa when it contradicts the transmitted madhhab text, but not when it contradicts a definitive shari text.

Ruling on Acting Upon the Weak Opinion in the Hanafi School
Rules for mufti and judge regarding weak/rajih opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a weak (marjuh) opinion may be acted upon in the Hanafi school and apply the rules to distinguish permissible from impermissible practice for muftis, judges, and laypersons.

Core Idea
A weak opinion is like abrogated: prohibited in fatwa, judgment, and even personal practice, except for necessity or qualified scholarly choice.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on acting upon weak or marjuh opinions. The classical poem sets a strict default: no acting upon the weak, no answering with it. Yet exceptions exist for necessity and for scholars with recognized knowledge. Later commentaries clarify the Hanafi position as even stronger than the Shafi'i, banning personal practice as well. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for the mufti to issue correct fatwas and for the judge to render valid judgments.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule from the poem
Identify the default prohibition: acting upon a weak opinion is impermissible, and it cannot be used to answer a questioner.
The poem's first lines: 'وَلَا يَجُوزُ بِالضَعِيف العَمَل وَلَا بِهِ يُجَابُ مَن جَاءَ يَسأل'
2
Identify the exceptions
The poem mentions two exceptions: (1) a person in necessity (dharura), and (2) a person with well-known knowledge (ma‘rifa mashhura). Understand that these apply to personal practice, not to giving fatwa or judgment.
The phrase 'إلا لِعَامِل لَه ضَرُورَه أَوْ مَنْ لَه مَعْرِفَةٌ مَشْهُورَه'
3
Distinguish the judge's absolute prohibition
The judge is explicitly forbidden from judging by a weak opinion. If he does, his judgment is void. Judges are bound by the rajih (preponderant) opinion of the school.
Line: 'لَكنَّمَا القَاضِي بِهِ لا يَقْضِي وَإِنْ قَضَى فَحُكمُه لَا يَمْضِى'
4
Understand the Hanafi position vs Shafi'i on personal practice
Imam al-Subki (Shafi'i) allowed acting upon a weak opinion for one's own practice. The Hanafi school, as stated by Shurunbulali, prohibits even for oneself, treating the marjuh as abrogated (mansukh).
The commentary quotes Shurunbulali: 'مذهب الحنفية : المنع عن المرجوح حتى لنفسه؛ لكون المرجوح صار منسوخاً'
5
Apply the rule to mufti and judge scenarios
A mufti must give fatwa according to the rajih. He may only mention the weak opinion in case of necessity, but cannot rely on it. A judge cannot use it at all. The author adds that the abrogation analogy holds when the issue originally had two opinions.
Consider the comment: 'التعليل بأنه صار منسوخا إنما يظهر فيما لو كان في المسألة قولان'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَجُوزُ بِالضَعِيف العَمَل وَلَا بِهِ يُجَابُ مَن جَاءَ يَسأل 
إلا لِعَامِل لَه ضَرُورَه أَوْ مَنْ لَه مَعْرِفَةٌ مَشْهُورَه 
لَكنَّمَا القَاضِي بِهِ لا يَقْضِي وَإِنْ قَضَى فَحُكمُه لَا يَمْضِى 
لا سِيمَا قُضَاتُنَا إِذْ قُيَدُوا بِرَاجِحِ الْمَذْهَبِ حِيْنَ قُلدوا

مطلب في الإفتاء والعمل على القول الضعيف

قدمنا أول الشرح عن العلامة قاسم : " أن الحكم والفتيا بما هو مرجوح خلاف الإجماع، وأن المرجوح في مقابلة الراجح بمنزلة العدم، والترجيح بغير مرجح في المتقابلات ممنوع ، وأن من يكتفى بأن يكون فتواه أو عمله موافقا لقول أو وجه في المسألة، ويعمل بما شاء من الأقوال والوجوه من غير نظر في الترجيح، فقد جهل وخرق الإجماع " . اهـ .
وقدمنا هناك نحوه عن فتاوى العلامة ابن حجر، لكن فيها أيضا : قال الإمام السبكي في الوقف من فتاويه : "يجوز تقليد الوجه الضعيف في نفس الأمر بالنسبة للعمل في حق نفسه لا في الفتوى والحكم، فقد نقل ابن الصلاح الإجماع على أنه لا يجوز " . اهـ .
وقال العلامة الشرنبلالي في رسالته "العقد الفريد في جواز التقليد " : مقتضى مذهب الشافعي كما قاله السبكي : منع العمل بالقول المرجوح في القضاء والإفتاء دون العمل لنفسه. ومذهب الحنفية : المنع عن المرجوح حتى لنفسه؛ لكون المرجوح صار منسوخاً " . اهـ . قلت : التعليل بأنه صار منسوخا إنما يظهر فيما لو كان في المسألة قولان، وإلا فلا.
It is not permissible to act upon the weak [opinion], nor should it be used to answer one who comes asking, except for one who has a necessity, or one who has a well-known [level of] knowledge. However, the judge should not judge by it, and if he judges, his judgment does not pass. Especially our judges, since they are restricted to the rajih of the school when they are appointed. 

Topic: On giving fatwa and acting upon the weak opinion.

We mentioned earlier in the commentary from Allamah Qasim: 'That judgment and fatwa based on the marjuh is contrary to consensus, and that the marjuh in comparison to the rajih is like non-existence, and tarjih without a preponderator in comparable matters is prohibited, and whoever is satisfied that his fatwa or action agrees with a saying or a perspective in the issue, and acts according to whatever he wishes of sayings and perspectives without considering tarjih, has become ignorant and broken the consensus.' End.

We mentioned similarly from the fatawa of Allamah Ibn Hajar, but in it also: Imam al-Subki said in his fatawa on waqf: 'It is permissible to follow the weak perspective in actuality with respect to action concerning oneself, not in fatwa and judgment, for Ibn al-Salah transmitted consensus that it is not permissible.' End.

Allamah Shurunbulali said in his treatise 'Al-‘Iqd al-Farid fi Jawaz al-Taqleed': 'The implication of the Shafi'i school as al-Subki said: prohibition of acting upon the marjuh in judgment and fatwa, but not acting for oneself. The Hanafi school: prohibition of the marjuh even for oneself, because the marjuh becomes abrogated.' End. I said: The reasoning that it becomes abrogated is evident only if there were two opinions in the issue, otherwise not.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule delineating the limits of using a weak (da'if) or marjuh (preponderated) opinion in the Hanafi school. The poetic lines (70-74) set the general rule: acting upon a weak opinion is impermissible, and it cannot be used to answer a questioner. Exceptions are made for two cases: (1) a person in dire need (dharura), and (2) a scholar with well-known knowledge who, through his own ijtihad, finds the weak opinion to be strong. However, a judge is absolutely forbidden from adjudicating by a weak opinion; if he does, the judgment is void. The judge is bound by the rajih (preponderant) opinion of the school. The commentary then expands on this. Allamah Qasim states that using the marjuh in fatwa or judgment violates consensus (ijma'). The marjuh is treated as non-existent. One cannot pick and choose opinions without reason. Then, a nuance is introduced: Imam al-Subki (Shafi'i) held that a person may follow a weak opinion for his own personal practice, but not in fatwa or judgment. However, Shurunbulali clarifies that the Hanafi position is stricter: the marjuh is forbidden even for one's own practice, likening it to abrogated (mansukh) rulings. The author (Ibn Abidin?) discusses the reasoning, noting that the abrogation analogy holds only when the issue originally had two conflicting views; otherwise, it is not literal abrogation. Overall, the Hanafi mufti must issue fatwa according to the rajih, and acting on a weak opinion is restricted to necessity or scholarly ijtihad, never for judges.

Key Terms
القول الضعيف (al-qawl al-da'if): An opinion that is weak or preponderated (marjuh) in the school, as opposed to the sahih/rajih.
المرجوح (al-marjuh): The preponderated opinion, i.e., the weaker side after tarjih.
الراجح (al-rajih): The preponderant opinion that is to be followed in fatwa and qada.
الضرورة (al-darura): Necessity that permits acting upon a weak opinion, such as extreme hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, can a judge issue a ruling based on a weak (marjuh) opinion?
A) Yes, if he finds it more convincing.
B) Yes, but only in cases of necessity.
C) No, it is absolutely prohibited; his judgment would be void.
D) No, unless he is a mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
A weak (marjuh) opinion in the Hanafi school is like abrogated: prohibited for fatwa, judgment, and even personal practice, except in genuine necessity or for a qualified scholar acting privately.

8 min
Ruling on Issuing Fatwa and Acting upon Weak Opinions
Explaining scholarly dispute on permissibility of acting upon weak opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on acting upon weak opinions and apply the rule to distinguish between permissible and prohibited scenarios in mufti practice.

Core Idea
A weak opinion in the presence of a strong one is like non-existent; acting upon it is prohibited for both fatwa and personal practice in the Hanafi school.

This passage addresses a critical rule in rasm al-mufti: the permissibility of acting upon weak (marju) opinions. The author begins by stating a consensus that issuing fatwa and acting upon weak opinions is prohibited. However, a Shafi'i position reported by Imam al-Subki allows personal action on weak opinions, raising an apparent contradiction. Understanding the reconciliation is essential for a mufti to correctly apply preponderance and avoid arbitrary selection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term: al-marju (weak opinion)
Recognize that al-marju is the weaker opinion in a fiqh issue that has been outweighed by a stronger opinion (rajih). In Hanafi usul, it is treated as legally non-existent.
The text states: 'المرجوح في مقابل الراجح بمنزلة العدم' (the weak in the presence of the strong is like non-existence).
2
State the Hanafi rule
The Hanafi school prohibits acting upon a weak opinion even for one's own personal practice, because the weak opinion is considered like abrogated. This is stricter than the Shafi'i position.
The passage quotes al-Shurunbulali: 'مَذْهَبُ الْحَنَفِيَّةِ: الْمَنْعُ عَنِ الْمَرْجُوحِ حَتَّى لِنَفْسِهِ؛ لِكَوْنِ الْمَرْجُوحِ صَارَ مَنْسُوخاً'
3
Analyze the Shafi'i counter-position
Imam al-Subki (Shafi'i) permits following a weak opinion for personal action, but not for fatwa or judgment. This creates an apparent conflict with the claimed consensus.
The text reports Subki: 'يَجُوزُ تَقْلِيدُ الْوَجْهِ الضَّعِيفِ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلْعَمَلِ فِي حَقِّ نَفْسِهِ لَا فِي الْفَتْوَى وَالْحُكْمِ'
4
Understand the author's reconciliation
The author (Ibn Abidin) resolves the apparent contradiction by interpreting Subki's allowance as limited to necessity, while the consensus prohibits unrestricted choice without preponderance.
He says: 'الْأَظْهَرُ فِي الْجَوَابِ ... إِنَّ الْإِجْمَاعَ عَلَى مَنْعِ إِطْلَاقِ التَّخْيِيرِ'
5
Apply to mufti's practice
A Hanafi mufti must never base a fatwa on a weak opinion unless genuine necessity (darura) exists. Even then, caution is required, as the weak opinion is like abrogated.
The rule from the passage: 'أَمَّا لَوْ عَمِلَ بِالضَّعِيفِ فِي بَعْضِ الْأَوْقَاتِ لِضَرُورَةٍ اقْتَضَتْ ذَلِكَ، فَلَا يُمْنَعُ مِنْهُ'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْإِفْتَاءُ وَالْعَمَلُ عَلَى الضَّعِيفِ خِلَافُ الْإِجْمَاعِ، وَأَنَّ الْمَرْجُوحَ فِي مُقَابَلَةِ الرَّاجِحِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْعَدَمِ، وَالتَّرْجِيحُ بِغَيْرِ مُرَجِّحٍ فِي الْمُتَقَابِلَاتِ مَمْنُوعٌ، وَأَنَّ مَنْ يَكْتَفِي بِأَنْ تَكُونَ فَتْوَاهُ أَوْ عَمَلُهُ مُوَافِقاً لِقَوْلٍ أَوْ وَجْهٍ فِي الْمَسْأَلَةِ، وَيَعْمَلُ بِمَا شَاءَ مِنَ الْأَقْوَالِ وَالْوُجُوهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ نَظَرٍ فِي التَّرْجِيحِ، فَقَدْ جَهِلَ وَخَرَقَ الْإِجْمَاعَ". اهـ. وَقَدَّمْنَا هُنَاكَ نَحْوَهُ عَنْ فَتَاوَى الْعَلَّامَةِ ابْنِ حَجَرٍ، لَكِنَّ فِيهَا أَيْضاً(١): قَالَ الْإِمَامُ السُّبْكِيُّ فِي الْوَقْفِ مِنْ فَتَاوِيهِ: "يَجُوزُ تَقْلِيدُ الْوَجْهِ الضَّعِيفِ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلْعَمَلِ فِي حَقِّ نَفْسِهِ لَا فِي الْفَتْوَى وَالْحُكْمِ، فَقَدْ نَقَلَ ابْنُ الصَّلَاحِ الْإِجْمَاعَ عَلَى أَنَّهُ لَا يَجُوزُ". اهـ. وَقَالَ الْعَلَّامَةُ الشُّرُنْبُلَالِيُّ فِي رِسَالَتِهِ "الْعِقْدُ الْفَرِيدُ فِي جَوَازِ التَّقْلِيدِ": مُقْتَضَى مَذْهَبِ الشَّافِعِيِّ كَمَا قَالَهُ السُّبْكِيُّ: مَنْعُ الْعَمَلِ بِالْقَوْلِ الْمَرْجُوحِ فِي الْقَضَاءِ وَالْإِفْتَاءِ دُونَ الْعَمَلِ لِنَفْسِهِ. وَمَذْهَبُ الْحَنَفِيَّةِ: الْمَنْعُ عَنِ الْمَرْجُوحِ حَتَّى لِنَفْسِهِ؛ لِكَوْنِ الْمَرْجُوحِ صَارَ مَنْسُوخاً". اهـ. قُلْتُ (٢): التَّعْلِيلُ بِأَنَّهُ صَارَ مَنْسُوخاً إِنَّمَا يَظْهَرُ فِيمَا لَوْ كَانَ فِي الْمَسْأَلَةِ قَوْلَانِ، رَجَعَ الْمُجْتَهِدُ عَنْ أَحَدِهِمَا، أَوْ عُلِمَ تَأَخُّرُ أَحَدِهِمَا عَنِ الْآخَرِ، وَإِلَّا فَلَا، كَمَا لَوْ كَانَ فِي الْمَسْأَلَةِ قَوْلٌ لِأَبِي يُوسُفَ وَقَوْلٌ لِمُحَمَّدٍ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَظْهَرُ فِيهِ النَّسْخُ، لَكِنَّ مُرَادَهُ (١) أَنَّهُ إِذَا صَحَّحَ أَحَدَهُمَا، صَارَ الْآخَرُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمَنْسُوخِ. وَهُوَ مَعْنَى مَا مَرَّ مِنْ قَوْلِ الْعَلَّامَةِ قَاسِمٍ: إِنَّ الْمَرْجُوحَ فِي مُقَابَلَةِ الرَّاجِحِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْعَدَمِ". ثُمَّ إِنَّ مَا ذَكَرَهُ السُّبْكِيُّ مِنْ جَوَازِ الْعَمَلِ بِالْمَرْجُوحِ فِي حَقِّ نَفْسِهِ عِنْدَ الشَّافِعِيِّ، مُخَالِفٌ لِمَا مَرَّ عَنِ الْعَلَّامَةِ قَاسِمٍ، وَقَدَّمْنَا مِثْلَهُ أَوَّلَ الشَّرْحِ عَنْ فَتَاوَى ابْنِ حَجَرٍ" مِنْ نَقْلِ الْإِجْمَاعِ عَلَى عَدَمِ الْإِفْتَاءِ وَالْعَمَلِ بِمَا شَاءَ مِنَ الْأَقْوَالِ، إِلَّا أَنْ يُقَالَ: الْمُرَادُ بِالْعَمَلِ الْحُكْمُ وَالْقَضَاءُ، وَهُوَ بَعِيدٌ (٣). وَالْأَظْهَرُ فِي الْجَوَابِ أَخْذاً مِنَ التَّعْبِيرِ بِالتَّشَهِّي أَنْ يُقَالَ: إِنَّ الْإِجْمَاعَ عَلَى مَنْعِ إِطْلَاقِ التَّخْيِيرِ، أَيْ بِأَنْ يَخْتَارَ وَيَتَشَهَّى مَهْمَا أَرَادَ مِنَ الْأَقْوَالِ فِي أَيِّ وَقْتٍ أَرَادَ، أَمَّا لَوْ عَمِلَ بِالضَّعِيفِ فِي بَعْضِ الْأَوْقَاتِ لِضَرُورَةٍ اقْتَضَتْ ذَلِكَ، فَلَا يُمْنَعُ مِنْهُ.
(١) قوله : "لكن فيها أيضاً استدراك على ما نقل عن العلامة قاسم من الحنفية والعلامة ابن حجر الهيثمى من الشافعية من أن العمل على المرجوح الضعيف خلاف الإجماع. ووجه الاستدراك أن الهيثمي نفسه نقل في فتاواه عن السبكي الشافعي جواز العمل على الضعيف لنفسه حتى جعل الشرنبلالي ذلك مقتضى مذهب الشوافع، فكيف يصح دعوى الإجماع ؟ وأجاب المصنف عنه بعد أسطر بأن الجواز المنقول عن السبكي عند الضرورة، والإجماع على المنع عند عدم الضرورة، فلا تناقض
(٢) قوله : قلت : " توجيه للفظ "المنسوخ في قول العلامة الشرنبلالي : لكون المرجوح صار منسوخا" ، حاصله : أنه قد يكون على معناه الحقيقي، وقد يطلق على ما هو بمنزلة المنسوخ مجازاً. وقوله : "وإلا فلا" أى وإن لم يكن في المسألة قولان فتعليل المنع عن العمل بالمرجوح لصيرورته منسوخا ، غير ظاهر، إلا أن يراد بالمنسوخ ما هو كالمنسوخ
(١) قوله : لكن مراده " أى مراد الشرنبلالي. وهذا تأويل من المصنف لتصحيح تعليل الشرنبلالي الذي علل به مذهب الأحناف.
(٢) قوله : "ثم إن ما ذكره السبكي " بيان للتعارض بين قول السبكي، وبين كلام العلامة قاسم وابن حجر الهيثمي، فإن مقتضى كلامه أن العمل بالضعيف يجوز في حق نفسه، وإنما المنع فى حق الإفتاء لغيره والقضاء به، والظاهر من كلامهما أن العمل على الضعيف مثل الإفتاء والقضاء به في المنع بالإجماع . ثم وفق المصنف بين كلامهم ،بوجهين ضعيف ، وقوى، فالضعيف حمل لفظ "العمل" على القضاء، والقوى حمل الجواز على الضرورة، وعدمه على عدمها .
(٣) قوله : " وهو بعيد " لأنهم اتفقوا على أن القضاء كالإفتاء، وأنه لا يجوز للقاضي أن يحكم إلا بما هو الراجح عنده كما لا يجوز للمفتى أن يفتى إلا بالراجح عنده، كما سيذكره المصنف عن العلامة ،قاسم عن أبي العباس أحمد بن إدريس المالكي القرافي رحمه الله تعالى .
Issuing fatwa and acting upon the weak (opinion) is contrary to consensus, and that the marju (weaker opinion) in the presence of the rajih (stronger opinion) is like non-existence, and giving preponderance without a preponderant factor among opposites is prohibited, and that one who is satisfied that his fatwa or action is in agreement with a statement or aspect in the matter, and acts according to whatever he wishes of statements and aspects without considering preponderance, indeed he is ignorant and violates consensus. End. And we have already presented something similar from the fatawa of Allamah Ibn Hajar, but in it also (1): Imam Subki said in his fatawa on endowment: "It is permissible to follow the weak aspect in reality with respect to action for one's own self, not in fatwa and judgment, for Ibn al-Salah has transmitted consensus that it is not permissible." End. And Allamah Shurunbulali said in his treatise "al-Aqd al-Farid fi Jawaz al-Taqlid": The implication of the Shafi'i school, as Subki said, is prohibition of acting upon the weak opinion in judgment and fatwa, but not for one's own action. And the Hanafi school: prohibition of the weak opinion even for one's own self, because the weak opinion has become abrogated. End. I say (2): The reasoning that it has become abrogated is only apparent when there are two sayings in the case, the mujtahid retracted one of them, or it is known that one is later than the other; otherwise no, as when there is a saying of Abu Yusuf and a saying of Muhammad in the case, for abrogation does not appear therein, but his intention (1) is that when one of them is deemed correct, the other becomes like abrogated. And that is the meaning of what has passed from the statement of Allamah Qasim: Indeed the weaker in the presence of the stronger is like non-existence. Then what Subki mentioned about the permissibility of acting upon the weak for one's own self according to the Shafi'i school is contrary to what has passed from Allamah Qasim, and we have presented its like at the beginning of the commentary from the fatawa of Ibn Hajar, from the transmission of consensus on the prohibition of issuing fatwa and acting upon whatever he wishes of statements, unless it is said: by 'action' is meant judgment and adjudication, and that is far-fetched (3). And the more apparent in the answer, taking from the expression 'literally desiring', is to say: Indeed consensus is on the prohibition of absolute choice, i.e., that he chooses and desires whatever he wants of statements at any time he wants. As for if he acts upon the weak at some times due to a necessity that requires that, then he is not prevented from it.
(1) He said: "But in it also" is an istidrak (critical remark) on what was transmitted from Allamah Qasim of the Hanafis and Allamah Ibn Hajar al-Haytami of the Shafi'is, that acting upon the weak marju is contrary to consensus. And the aspect of the istidrak is that al-Haytami himself transmitted in his fatawa from al-Subki al-Shafi'i the permissibility of acting upon the weak for oneself, such that Shurunbulali made that the implication of the Shafi'i school. So how can the claim of consensus be valid? And the author answered him after a few lines that the permission transmitted from Subki is in case of necessity, and consensus on prohibition is in case of no necessity, so there is no contradiction.
(2) He said: I say: This is a clarification of the word 'abrogated' in the statement of Allamah Shurunbulali: 'because the weak has become abrogated.' Its summary: that it may be in its real meaning, and it may be applied metaphorically to what is like abrogated. And his statement "otherwise no" means: if there are not two sayings in the case, then the reasoning for prohibition by its being abrogated is not apparent, unless by 'abrogated' is meant what is like abrogated.
(1) He said: "but his intention" i.e., the intention of Shurunbulali. And this is an interpretation by the author to correct Shurunbulali's reasoning with which he justified the Hanafi school.
(2) He said: "Then what Subki mentioned" is an explanation of the contradiction between Subki's statement and the statement of Allamah Qasim and Ibn Hajar al-Haytami, for the implication of Subki's statement is that acting upon the weak is permissible for one's own self, and the prohibition is only in fatwa for others and judgment. And the apparent from the statements of Qasim and Ibn Hajar is that acting upon the weak is like issuing fatwa and judging by it in being prohibited by consensus. Then the author reconciled between their statements with two ways: weak and strong. The weak is to apply the word 'action' to judgment; the strong is to apply permission to necessity and prohibition to its absence.
(3) He said: "And it is far-fetched" because they agreed that judgment is like fatwa, and it is not permissible for a judge to judge except by what is preponderant in his view, just as it is not permissible for a mufti to issue fatwa except by what is preponderant in his view, as the author will mention from Allamah Qasim from Abu al-Abbas Ahmad ibn Idris al-Maliki al-Qarafi, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage addresses a critical rule in rasm al-mufti: the permissibility of acting upon or issuing fatwas based on weak (marju) opinions. The author begins by stating a consensus: issuing fatwa and acting upon weak opinions is prohibited. The weak opinion in the presence of a strong one is considered null. Choosing without a preponderant factor is forbidden. One who picks any opinion without considering preponderance is ignorant and violates consensus. This is the established position attributed to Allamah Qasim (Hanafi) and Ibn Hajar al-Haytami (Shafi'i). However, the author notes a citation from Ibn Hajar's fatawa where Imam al-Subki (Shafi'i) permits following a weak opinion for one's personal action, not for fatwa or judgment. Then Shurunbulali's treatise is quoted: he states that according to the Shafi'i school, acting upon a weak opinion is prohibited in judgment and fatwa but allowed for oneself; whereas the Hanafi school prohibits it even for oneself, because the weak opinion has become like abrogated. The author (likely the commentator, Ibn Abidin) then provides his own analysis. He clarifies that the analogy to abrogation applies only when there are two opinions in the case and the mujtahid retracted one or it is later, e.g., if there is a saying of Abu Yusuf and a saying of Muhammad, abrogation is not literal, but if one is deemed correct, the other becomes like abrogated. This aligns with Qasim's statement that the weak is like non-existent. The author then highlights a contradiction: Subki allows personal action, while Qasim and Ibn Hajar claim consensus on absolute prohibition. He resolves it by interpreting Subki's allowance as limited to necessity, whereas the consensus is against unrestricted choice. He prefers this reconciliation: consensus prohibits absolute free choice, but acting upon a weak opinion due to necessity is not prevented. The passage also includes marginal notes explaining the subtleties of the term 'abrogated' and the reconciliation of apparent contradictions.

Key Terms
الْمَرْجُوح (al-marjūḥ): The weaker opinion in a fiqh issue that has been outweighed by a stronger opinion (rājiḥ). In this text, it is considered like non-existent or abrogated.
النَّسْخ (al-naskh): Abrogation; used metaphorically to describe the status of a weaker opinion once a stronger opinion is established, as if it were abrogated.
الْإِفْتَاءُ وَالْعَمَلُ عَلَى الضَّعِيفِ (al-iftā' wa al-'amal 'alā al-ḍa'īf): Issuing a formal legal response (fatwa) or personally acting upon a weak (ḍa'īf) or outweighed (marjūḥ) opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as stated in this passage, what is the ruling on acting upon a weak (marju) opinion for one's own personal practice?
A) Permissible, as long as one does not issue fatwa to others
B) Prohibited, because the weak opinion is considered like abrogated
C) Permissible only in cases of necessity, otherwise prohibited
D) Recommended, as it shows tawassu' (flexibility)

Key Takeaway
A weak opinion that is outweighed by a strong one is legally considered non-existent; therefore, it cannot be acted upon or used for fatwa, even for oneself, in the Hanafi school.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
advanced
8 min
Acting on Weak Opinions in Fatwa
Ruling on acting on weak opinions in Hanafi fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between prohibited tashahhi and permissible necessity-based action on weak opinions in Hanafi fatwa.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school prohibits arbitrary selection (tashahhi) of weak opinions but permits them when necessity demands.

This passage addresses a critical question in Hanafi ifta: can a mufti ever give a fatwa based on a weaker opinion (marjūḥ) instead of the preponderant view (rājiḥ)? The text navigates between two extremes—absolute prohibition reported as consensus and apparent permission by some Shafi'i scholars. Through careful reconciliation, the Hanafi position emerges: while tashahhī (whimsical picking) is banned, necessity (ḍarūrah) can justify acting on a weaker view. You will learn how this principle operates through the example of a traveler following Abu Yusuf's ruling on ghusl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem class
The passage begins by distinguishing cases where abrogation (naskh) is evident from those where it is not—like when Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree. Note that without clear later opinion, we treat one as 'like abrogated' if deemed more correct.
This sets the stage for the weaker opinion being effectively non-existent in normal cases.
2
Extract the core principle
From 'Allama Qasim: 'al-marjūḥ fī muqābalat al-rājiḥ bi-manzilat al-`adam' (the weak in contrast to the strong is like non-existence). This establishes the default ruling: weak opinions have no operative value for fatwa.
This is a key qāʿidah in Hanafi iftāʾ.
3
Analyze the competing view
Al-Subki (Shafi'i) is cited as allowing action on weak opinions for oneself. This appears to contradict the Hanafi consensus reported by Ibn Hajar. List both positions clearly.
The contradiction forces a reconciliation.
4
Apply the reconciliation
The consensus only prohibits absolute tashahhī (arbitrary choice at any time). Acting on a weak opinion for a genuine necessity (ḍarūrah) is not tashahhī and is therefore permitted. Identify the key nuance: prohibition of whim, not of need.
This saves both the consensus and al-Subki's allowance.
5
Test with the case example
Al-Shurnbalali's example: traveler/guest who fears suspicion may follow Abu Yusuf's weaker opinion that ghusl is not obligatory if he held his penis during wet dream until desire subsided. Confirm that necessity triggers the exception.
The example concretizes the abstract principle.

الإفتاء والعمل على الضعيف

قولان، رجع المجتهد عن أحدهما ، أو علم تأخر أحدهما عن الآخر، وإلا فلا، كما لو كان في المسألة قول لأبي يوسف وقول لمحمد، فإنه لا يظهر فيه النسخ، لكن مراده أنه إذا صحح أحدهما ، صار الآخر بمنزلة المنسوخ. وهو معنى ما مر من قول العلامة قاسم : إن المرجوح في مقابلة الراجح بمنزلة العدم. ثم إن ما ذكره السبكي من جواز العمل بالمرجوح في حق نفسه عند الشافعى، مخالف لما مر عن العلامة قاسم، وقدمنا مثله أول الشرح عن فتاوى ابن حجر من نقل الإجماع على عدم الإفتاء والعمل بما شاء من الأقوال، إلا أن يقال : المراد بالعمل الحكم والقضاء، وهو بعيد. والأظهر فى الجواب أخذا من التعبير بالتشهى أن يقال : إن الإجماع على منع إطلاق التخيير ، أى بأن يختار ويتشهى مهما أراد من الأقوال فى أى وقت أراد، أما لو عمل بالضعيف في بعض الأوقات لضرورة اقتضت ذلك، فلا يمنع منه. وعليه يحمل ما تقدم عن الشرنبلالي: "من أن مذهب الحنفية المنع ، بدليل أنهم أجازوا للمسافر والضيف الذى خاف الريبة أن يأخذ بقول أبي يوسف بعدم وجوب الغسل على المحتلم الذى أمسك ذكره عند ما أحس بالاحتلام إلى أن فترت شهوته ثم أرسله، مع أن قوله هذا خلاف الراجح في المذهب، لكن أجازوا الأخذ به للضرورة."
Fatwa and Acting on the Weak [Opinion]. Two statements: the mujtahid retracted one of them, or he knows that one is later than the other, otherwise not, as if there is a statement of Abu Yusuf and a statement of Muhammad in a case, there is no abrogation, but his intent is that if he corrected one, the other becomes like abrogated. This is the meaning of what passed from 'Allama Qasim: the weak in contrast to the strong is like non-existence. Then what al-Subki mentioned about the permissibility of acting on the weak for oneself according to al-Shafi'i contradicts what passed from 'Allama Qasim, and we presented similar at the beginning of the commentary from the fatawa of Ibn Hajar regarding the transmission of consensus on the prohibition of issuing fatwa and acting on whichever opinions one wishes, unless it is said: the meaning of 'acting' is judgment and judiciary, and that is unlikely. The most apparent in the answer, taking from the expression 'whimsical choice', is that the consensus is on the prohibition of absolute choice, i.e., that one selects and whimsically chooses whichever opinions one wants at any time. As for acting on the weak in some times due to necessity that requires it, it is not prohibited. And upon that is carried what preceded from al-Shurnbalali: 'The Hanafi school forbids, as evidenced by their allowing the traveler and the guest who fears suspicion to take the opinion of Abu Yusuf regarding the non-obligation of ghusl for the one who experienced a wet dream and held his penis when he felt it until his desire subsided and then released it, even though this opinion contradicts the preponderant view in the school, but they allowed it for necessity.'
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the permissibility of acting upon weaker opinions in the Hanafi school. The author begins by distinguishing cases where abrogation (naskh) is evident (if the mujtahid retracts or one opinion is known to be later) from cases where it is not, such as when there is a statement from Abu Yusuf and another from Muhammad. In the latter, there is no apparent abrogation, but if one opinion is deemed more correct (sahhah), the other becomes like an abrogated ruling. This is consistent with 'Allama Qasim's statement that the weak is like non-existent in contrast to the strong. The author then notes a conflict: al-Subki (Shafi'i) allowed acting on a weak opinion for oneself, whereas 'Allama Qasim and Ibn Hajar reported consensus against both fatwa and action based on any chosen opinion. The author attempts reconciliation: the consensus only prohibits absolute arbitrary choice (tashahhi), i.e., selecting any opinion at any time without reason. However, acting on a weak opinion in a particular instance due to necessity is not prohibited. This interpretation is supported by al-Shurnbalali's example: the Hanafis allowed a traveler or guest who fears suspicion (of impurity) to follow Abu Yusuf's view that ghusl is not required if he held his penis during a wet dream until desire subsided. This view is weaker but was allowed due to necessity. Thus, the school's general prohibition applies to whimsical selection, not to genuine need.

Key Terms
المرجوح (al-marjūḥ): The weaker opinion in a school, in contrast to the preponderant (rājiḥ)
التشهي (al-tashahhī): Whimsical selection of any opinion without justification
الضعيف (al-ḍaʿīf): The weaker opinion, equivalent to marjūḥ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is it permitted to act on a weak opinion in the Hanafi school?
A) Whenever the mufti prefers it
B) Only if it is the preponderant view
C) In cases of necessity such as travel or fear of suspicion
D) Never, because it is prohibited by consensus

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits tashahhī (whimsical selection) of weak opinions but permits them for genuine necessity.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Acting on Weak Opinions Under Necessity: The Hanafi Rule
Justification for acting on weak opinions with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a Hanafi mufti may issue a fatwa based on a weak (da'if) opinion and apply them to a case of necessity.

Core Idea
A weak opinion from a qualified mujtahid may be followed under genuine necessity to remove hardship.

This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti addresses a critical question: when can a Hanafi mufti step outside the preponderant (rajih) view of the school? The author clarifies that the general prohibition is against caprice (tashahhi), not necessity (darurah). Through examples from al-Marghinani and others, we learn that weak opinions from authoritative imams can be used to ease hardship for the people.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its exception
The Hanafi school generally prohibits acting on weak opinions, but this is restricted to cases of personal caprice (tashahhi). The exception is genuine necessity (darurah).
2
Examine the examples of necessity
The author gives three examples: the traveler/guest taking Abu Yusuf's opinion on ghusl, the chronic wound case (kayy al-himmasah), and the menstrual blood case. These show that weak opinions are permitted when following the strong opinion causes hardship.
3
Analyze the status of al-Marghinani's opinion
Al-Marghinani's opinion that blood that does not flow at the time of exit does not nullify wudu is considered shadhdh (weak) but is from a mujtahid of high rank (takhrij and tas-hih). Therefore, it can be followed under necessity.
The opinion is not rejected; it is valid but less strong.
4
Determine the conditions for acting on a weak opinion
Conditions include: genuine necessity, the weak opinion must be from a qualified mujtahid (like al-Marghinani), and the mufti must seek ease (taysir) for the people.

شرح عقود رسم المفتی تابعه عليه بعد المراجعة الكثيرة، فهو قول شاذ. ولكن صاحب الهداية " إمام جليل من أعظم مشايخ المذهب من طبقة أصحاب التخريج والتصحيح، كما مر، فيجوز للمعذور تقليده فى هذا القول عند الضرورة، فإن فيه توسعة عظيمة لأهل الأعذار ، كما بينته في رسالتي المسماة " الأحكام المخصصة بكى
الحمصة".
وقد كنت ابتليت مدة بكى الحمّصة (1) ، ولم أجد ما تصح به صلاتي على مذهبنا بلا مشقة إلا على هذا القول ؛ لأن الخارج منه وإن كان قليلا لكنه لو ترك يسيل، وهو نجس وناقض للطهارة على القول المشهور، خلافا لما قاله بعضهم، كما قد بينته في الرسالة المذكورة، ولا يصير به صاحب عذر ؛ لأنه يمكن دفع العذر بالغسل والربط بنحو جلدة مانعة للسيلان عند كل صلاة ، كما كنت أفعله، ولكن فيه مشقة وحرج عظيم فاضطررت إلى تقليد هذا القول، ثم لما عافاني الله تعالى منه أعدت صلاة تلك المدة . والله تعالى الحمد.

Commentary on the Contracts of the Mufti (1) 200 Issuing Fatwa and Acting on the Weak Opinion And it is interpreted (i.e., the prohibition attributed to the Hanafis) as referring to caprice, as indicated by the Hanafi jurists' permission to act on weak opinions in some cases. The author mentions three: the issue of nocturnal emission, cauterization with a chickpea, and menstrual blood. (2) The interpretation of the earlier statement from al-Shurunbulali: 'The Hanafi school prohibits it, evidenced by their permitting the traveler and the guest who fears suspicion to take the opinion of Abu Yusuf that ghusl is not obligatory on the one who has a wet dream who held his penis when he felt the emission until his desire subsided and then released it, even though this opinion contradicts the preponderant view in the school, but they permitted it due to necessity.' (2) And of this kind should be what Imam al-Marghinani, author of al-Hidayah, mentioned in his book Mukhtarat al-Nawazil—a famous book quoted by commentators of al-Hidayah and others—where he said in the chapter on impurity: 'Blood that comes out from sores little by little without flowing, that is not a preventer even if it is much. And it is said: if it were such that if left it would flow, it prevents.' Then he repeated the issue in the nullifiers of wudu, saying: 'If something little comes out and he wipes it with a cloth, even if left it would flow, it does not nullify. And it is said: ...' etc. I have checked another copy and found the expression to be likewise. It is not hidden that the famous view in most books of the school is the second opinion expressed as 'it is said.' As for what he chose, the first opinion, I have not seen anyone preceding him in it, nor anyone following him after extensive review. It is a weak (shadhdh) opinion. But the author of al-Hidayah is a great imam from the greatest scholars of the school from the class of those qualified for takhrij and tas-hih, as mentioned, so it is permissible for the excused person to follow him in this opinion when necessary, for in it is great ease for people with excuses, as I explained in my treatise titled 'al-Ahkam al-Mukhasasah bi Kayy al-Himmasah.' I myself was afflicted for a time with cauterization of the chickpea, and I found no way to make my prayer valid in our school without difficulty except according to this opinion, because what comes out, though little, if left would flow, and it is impure and nullifies purification according to the famous opinion, contrary to what some said, as I explained in that treatise. And he does not become a person with an excuse because the excuse can be avoided by washing and binding with something like a skin that prevents flow before each prayer, as I used to do, but that involves great hardship and difficulty. So I was compelled to follow this opinion, and then when Allah cured me of it, I repeated the prayers of that period. Praise be to Allah. And the author of al-Bahr mentioned in the chapter on menstruation, in the discussion of colors of blood, weak opinions, then said: 'In al-Mi'raj from Fakhr al-A'imma: If a mufti gives a fatwa with any of these opinions in places of necessity seeking ease, it is good.'
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage from 'Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti' addresses a critical methodological rule: when is it permissible for a mufti to issue a fatwa based on a weak (da'if or shadhdh) opinion within the Hanafi school? The author begins by clarifying that the Hanafi prohibition against acting on weak opinions is restricted to cases of personal caprice (tashahhi), not to cases of genuine necessity. He supports this by citing three examples from Hanafi fatwa practice: the traveler/guest who takes Abu Yusuf's opinion on ghusl after a wet dream, the case of blood from a wound treated with cauterization (kayy al-himmasah), and the case of menstrual blood colors. The core discussion focuses on the opinion of Imam al-Marghinani (author of al-Hidayah) in his work 'Mukhtarat al-Nawazil' regarding blood from wounds. Al-Marghinani chose the view that slow, non-flowing blood does not prevent prayer or nullify wudu, even if it would flow if left. This opinion is considered shadhdh (odd/weak) compared to the famous view that flowing blood does prevent. However, the author argues that due to al-Marghinani's high rank (he is from the class of mujtahidun fi al-takhrij wa al-tas-hih), his opinion can be followed by someone with an excuse (ma'dhur) when necessary. The author personally testifies to using this opinion when afflicted with a chronic wound that made it extremely difficult to maintain purity according to the famous view. After recovery, he repeated the prayers as a precaution. The passage concludes by citing a similar ruling from Sahib al-Bahr and Fakhr al-A'imma: it is commendable for a mufti to give fatwa with weak opinions in places of necessity seeking ease for the people. The key lesson is that the mufti is not strictly bound to the preponderant (rajih) opinion in all circumstances; necessity and hardship create a legitimate space for acting on weaker views, provided certain conditions are met (the opinion comes from a qualified imam, the necessity is genuine, and the mufti seeks ease for the people).

Key Terms
الضعيف (al-da'if): An opinion that is not preponderant (rajih) in the school, often considered shadhdh (odd) but still from a qualified mujtahid.
التشهي (al-tashahhi): Personal caprice or following one's desire without legitimate necessity.
كى الحمصة (kayy al-himmasah): A medical treatment using a chickpea-like substance to cauterize wounds that continuously ooze blood or pus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is it permissible for a Hanafi mufti to give a fatwa based on a weak opinion?
A) Whenever the mufti prefers a different ruling
B) Only when the weak opinion is from a mujtahid and there is genuine necessity seeking ease
C) Never, because weak opinions are always prohibited
D) Only if the weak opinion is from the Sahibayn

Key Takeaway
A weak opinion from a qualified Hanafi mujtahid may be acted upon under genuine necessity to remove hardship, provided the mufti seeks ease for the people.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Permissibility of Acting Upon Weak Opinions in Necessity
Ruling on when a mufti may use weak opinions due to necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a mufti may act upon a weak (da'if) opinion and apply the qawida 'يجوز العمل والإفتاء بالضعيف عند الاضطرار' to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A weak opinion becomes permissible when necessity arises, but only within strict bounds: no clear text, limited to need, and only while the excuse lasts.

This passage from 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti addresses a critical aspect of mufti methodology: when can a mufti or a layperson resort to a weak (non-mufta bihi) opinion? The author illustrates with a personal example and a case from menstruation, and then lays down the conditions for necessity. Understanding this ruling prevents misuse of concessions and preserves the integrity of the school's hierarchical reliance.

Guided Path
1
State the main qawida
Identify the central rule: 'It is permissible to act upon and give fatwa with the weak opinion in cases of necessity.' This is the explicit ruling in the passage.
2
Identify the three conditions of necessity
From the footnote, list the three conditions: (1) the case must not be in a place where a clear nass exists; (2) the permission is limited to the extent of the necessity; (3) it is valid only as long as the excuse or necessity persists.
These conditions are derived from the Hanafi legal maxim 'الضرورات تبيح المحظورات'.
3
Distinguish necessity from need
The passage differentiates darurah (necessity) from hajah (need). Necessity is stronger, temporary, and strictly measured; need is broader and allows more permanent concessions, like hiring for worship.
Both affect rulings, but only necessity permits weak opinions.
4
Apply the rule to the two furuat
Work through the two examples: (a) the case of kayy al-himmisah where a weak opinion allows ignoring discharge that breaks wudu, used under extreme hardship; (b) weak opinions on menstrual blood colors used for women with prolonged 'iddah.
Both show temporary reliance with later qada (repeat prayers).
5
Determine the mufti's limitation
The mufti may only give such a fatwa to one who is in necessity, not to the general public. The permission is not absolute but tied to the individual's circumstances.

الإفتاء والعمل على الضعيف
شرح عقود رسم المفتی تابعه عليه بعد المراجعة الكثيرة، فهو قول شاذ. ولكن صاحب الهداية " إمام جليل من أعظم مشايخ المذهب من طبقة أصحاب التخريج والتصحيح، كما مر، فيجوز للمعذور تقليده فى هذا القول عند الضرورة، فإن فيه توسعة عظيمة لأهل الأعذار ، كما بينته في رسالتي المسماة " الأحكام المخصصة بكى الحمصة".
وقد كنت ابتليت مدة بكى الحمّصة (1) ، ولم أجد ما تصح به صلاتي على مذهبنا بلا مشقة إلا على هذا القول ؛ لأن الخارج منه وإن كان قليلا لكنه لو ترك يسيل، وهو نجس وناقض للطهارة على القول المشهور، خلافا لما قاله بعضهم، كما قد بينته في الرسالة المذكورة، ولا يصير به صاحب عذر ؛ لأنه يمكن دفع العذر بالغسل والربط بنحو جلدة مانعة للسيلان عند كل صلاة ، كما كنت أفعله، ولكن فيه مشقة وحرج عظيم فاضطررت إلى تقليد هذا القول، ثم لما عافاني الله تعالى منه أعدت صلاة تلك المدة . والله تعالى الحمد.

وقد ذكر صاحب البحر في الحيض في بحث ألوان الدماء أقوالا ضعيفة، ثم قال : " وفى "المعراج عن فخر الأئمة : لو أفتى مفت بشيء من هذه الأقوال فى مواضع الضرورة (۲) طلباً للتيسير ، كان حسنا " . اهـ .
وتكاثره، وإنما ينظر إلى سيلانه عند خروجه ، فإن كان الخارج كثيراً يسيل بدون مهلة منع ، وإن كان يخرج شيئًا فشيئًا ، ثم يتكاثر فيسيل، لا يمنع . (مظ)

(۱) قوله : "بكى الحمصة الكي: هو الحسم بالحديد، والحمصة : نوع من الحبوب معروف، فيه قوة نشف الرطوبة، وأطباء الشام يربطونها على الجرح الذي لا يرقأ ؛ لتجذب رطوبته، وكثيراً ما يحتاج إلى هذا النوع من العلاج في الجرح الذي لا يزال الدم أو الصديد يسيل منه، وهذا الجرح ينشأ من ورم حاد في طرف الإصبع، ويسمى هذا المرض في العربية : " داحس " .
(۲) قوله : "في مواضع الضرورة أى بأن طالت عدتها فعالجت فرجها بدواء حتى صفرة فهى حيض وإن لم يكن في أيام حيضها .

متى يجوز العمل والإفتاء بالضعيف
مطلب
يجوز العمل والإفتاء بالضعيف عند الاضطرار
وبه علم أن المضطر له العمل بذلك لنفسه كما قلنا، وأن المفتى له الإفتاء به للمضطر . فما مر من أنه ليس له العمل بالضعيف ولا الإفتاء به، محمول على غير موضع الضرورة ) ، كما علمته من مجموع ما قررناه . والله تعالى أعلم . وينبغى أن يلحق بالضرورة أيضاً ما قدمناه من أنه لا يفتى بكفر مسلم في (۱) قوله : غير موضع الضرورة الضرورة في اللغة : شدة الحال، وهي اسم لمصدر الاضطرار والاضطرار معناه : الاحتياج إلى الشيء . واختلفت عبارات الفقهاء في تعريفها الاصطلاحية، وأجمع ما قيل فيه : " هي الحالة الملجئة لتناول الممنوع شرعاً " . ثم تعتبر بشروط ثلاثة أن لا تكون فى موضع ورد النص فيه، وأن تتقدر بقدرها : وأن تعتبر إلى بقاء العذر أو حالة الاضطرار، فإذا زال العذر أو حالة الاضطرار زالت الرخصة .
ثم هل هناك فرق بين الحاجة والضرورة أم لا؟ فجوابه : أن الفقهاء كثيراً ما يستعملونهما كأنهما مترادفين لكن الضرورة أشد باعثاً من الحاجة ؛ إذ مبنى الضرورة على ما لا بد للإنسان منه ، والحاجة تكون مبنية على التسهيل فيما يسع للإنسان تركه، وأيضًا ما جاز للضرورة يتقدر بقدرها بخلاف الحاجة، وكذلك ما أقيم للضرورة يكون موقتا بمدة قيام الضرورة ولا كذلك الحاجة، كجواز الاستئجار على الطاعات التي يخشى بالامتناع عنها تضييع القرآن ونحوه، فالحاجة دون الضرورة من هذه الجهات .
ثم هي تنقسم إلى مراتب أربعة ذكرها المحقق ابن الهمام في فتح القدير، وهى : الضرورة ،والحاجة، والزينة، والفضول. ولا أثر للمرتبتين الأخريين في تغير الأحكام. والله أعلم.
Issuing fatwa and acting upon the weak [opinion]. The commentary of 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: he follows it after much revision; it is a deviant (shadhdh) statement. But the author of al-Hidayah, a great imam from the greatest shaykhs of the school from the layer of the people of takhrij and tas'hih, as mentioned, so it is permissible for the excused person to follow him in this statement in cases of necessity, for therein is a great expansion for people with excuses, as I explained in my treatise named 'al-Ahkam al-Mukhassasah bi-Kayy al-Himmisah'. I was tested for a period with 'kayy al-himmisah', and I did not find anything by which my prayer would be valid in our school without hardship except this statement; because the discharge from it, though small, if left to flow, is impure and breaks ablution according to the well-known statement, contrary to what some said, as I explained in the aforementioned treatise, and he does not become a person with an excuse [for continuous bleeding] because it is possible to remove the excuse by washing and tying with something like a skin preventing the flow at every prayer, as I used to do, but there was great hardship and difficulty, so I was compelled to follow this statement. Then when Allah cured me from it, I repeated the prayers of that period. Praise be to Allah. 6. And the author of al-Bahr mentioned in [the chapter of] menstruation, in the discussion of colors of blood, weak statements, then said: 'In al-Mi'raj from Fakhr al-A'immah: if a mufti gives a fatwa with something from these statements in cases of necessity seeking ease, it would be good.' And its accumulation; rather consideration is given to its flowing at the time of exit: if the discharge is much and flows without pause, it prevents; and if it comes out little by little then accumulates and flows, it does not prevent. (maz) (1) His statement: 'kayy al-himmisah': al-kay is cauterization with iron, and al-himmisah is a type of known grain with a power to absorb moisture; physicians of Syria tie it on a wound that does not stop bleeding to absorb its moisture, and this type of treatment is often needed for a wound from which blood or pus keeps flowing; this wound arises from an acute tumor at the tip of the finger, called in Arabic 'dahis'. (2) His statement: 'in cases of necessity' meaning if her 'iddah is prolonged and she treated her private part with a medicine until [the discharge] becomes yellow, then it is menstruation even if it is not in her usual days. When is acting and giving fatwa with the weak permissible? A topic: Acting and giving fatwa with the weak is permissible in cases of necessity. And by this it is known that the one in necessity may act with it for himself as we said, and the mufti may give fatwa with it for the one in necessity. What has passed that he may not act with the weak nor give fatwa with it is carried upon [cases] other than necessity, as you know from the totality of what we have established. And Allah knows best. And it is fitting to also join to necessity what we have presented that a Muslim is not declared an unbeliever in... (1) His statement: other than the place of necessity: necessity in language is severity of condition, it is a noun from the masdar of idtirar, and idtirar means need for something. The expressions of jurists differ on its technical definition, and the most comprehensive is: 'It is a compelling state to consume what is legally prohibited.' Then it is considered with three conditions: that it not be in a place where a text has come, that it be measured by its extent, and that it be considered only as long as the excuse or state of necessity remains; if the excuse or state of necessity ceases, the concession ceases. Then: Is there a difference between need (hajah) and necessity (darurah)? The answer: The jurists often use them as if synonymous, but necessity is a stronger motive than need; because necessity is built upon what a person cannot do without, while need is built upon facilitation in what a person can afford to leave. Also, what is permitted due to necessity is measured by its extent unlike need, and likewise what is established for necessity is temporary for the duration of the necessity unlike need, such as the permissibility of hiring for acts of worship where refusing would lead to neglect of the Quran and the like. So need is below necessity from these aspects. Then it is divided into four ranks mentioned by al-Muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humann in Fath al-Qadir: necessity, need, adornment, and excess. The last two ranks have no effect on changing rulings. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a methodological ruling (rasm al-mufti) on when a mufti or a layperson may act upon or issue a fatwa based on a weak (da'if) opinion within the Hanafi school. The author first cites a specific case: the use of a weak opinion regarding a medical treatment called 'kayy al-himmisah' (cautery with a type of bean) for a wound that continuously oozes blood or pus. The well-known (mashhur) opinion holds that such discharge breaks wudu, but a weak opinion (attributed to the author of al-Hidayah) allows following it under necessity due to extreme hardship. The author shares his personal experience: he suffered from this condition, found it extremely difficult to wash and bind the wound before every prayer, so he followed the weak opinion temporarily, and after recovery he repeated the prayers. He then cites a similar case from al-Bahr regarding colors of menstrual blood, where weak opinions may be used in necessity. The key rule is stated explicitly: 'Acting and giving fatwa with the weak is permissible in cases of necessity (idtirar).' This is limited: the mufti may only give such a fatwa to one who is in necessity, and the person in necessity may act upon it only as long as the necessity lasts. The footnote qualifies 'necessity' with three conditions: (1) it must not be in a matter where a clear text (nass) exists, (2) it must be limited to the extent of the necessity, and (3) it is valid only as long as the excuse persists. The footnote further distinguishes necessity (darurah) from need (hajah): necessity is stronger, requires what one cannot avoid, and is temporary; need is for facilitation and is not as restrictive. Finally, necessity is ranked among four categories (necessity, need, adornment, excess), with only the first two affecting rulings. Thus, this passage teaches a nuanced principle: weak opinions are not generally permissible, but they become a valid recourse in genuine hardship, provided the strict conditions of necessity are met.

Key Terms
الضعيف (al-da'if): An opinion within the Hanafi school that is not the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position; it is a weak (shadhdh or marjuh) transmission.
الضرورة (al-darurah): A compelling state that forces one to take a legally prohibited matter; defined by the author as 'the compelling state to consume what is legally prohibited'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the three conditions for acting upon a weak opinion under necessity?
A) The weak opinion must be from a later fatwa, the necessity must be moderate, and the person must be a scholar.
B) The case must not conflict with a clear text, the necessity must be measured by its extent, and the permission lasts only as long as the excuse.
C) The weak opinion must be from the Hidayah, the necessity must be for prayer only, and the person must repeat all prayers later.
D) The weak opinion must be from a sahabi, the necessity must be extreme to the point of death, and the person must be a mufti.

Key Takeaway
Under genuine necessity, a mufti may resort to weak opinions, but only within the limits of no clear text, proportional to the need, and while the excuse lasts.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
6 min
Conditions for Acting and Giving Fatwa with Weak Narrations
Defines when weak narrations may be used in fatwa and action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a Hanafi mufti may act on or issue a fatwa using a weak narration, and distinguish between personal action and fatwa to others.

Core Idea
Weak narrations may be used only in necessity or for personal ijtihad, not for general fatwa.

This passage from Sharh Uqood Rasm al-Mufti addresses a critical question: When can a Hanafi mufti rely on a weak narration? The default is to follow authentic madhhab positions, but the author explains important exceptions rooted in the principle of easing hardship (raf' al-haraj). You will learn the precise conditions that permit the use of weak narrations and the crucial distinction between acting for oneself and giving fatwa to others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
State the default position: a mufti must follow authentic (sahih) narrations and established madhhab views. Weak narrations are not to be used generally.
The default is based on the principle that the madhhab's authorities have already weighed the evidences.
2
Recognize the exception for necessity
Identify that necessity (darurah) permits the use of weak narrations. Understand the three conditions: (1) no explicit text forbids it, (2) limited to the extent needed, (3) lasts only as long as the excuse.
The passage defines darurah as 'a state that compels one to take something legally prohibited.'
3
Distinguish between personal action and fatwa
Understand that a mujtahid may act on a weak narration for himself, but may only give fatwa using it to a person in necessity. For others, he must follow the madhhab.
This resolves the apparent contradiction in the texts.
4
Apply the hierarchy of necessity
Use Ibn al-Humam's four-tier classification: darurah, hajah, zinah, fudul. Only the first two affect rulings. Permissions due to darurah are temporary and restricted.
The passage emphasizes that 'need' (hajah) is less than necessity in strength and scope.
5
Determine applicability to the layperson
The layperson ('ammi) must follow authenticated positions and cannot apply weak narrations. Only the mujtahid in the madhhab may use them personally.
The layperson is excluded from the permission because he lacks the ability to weigh evidences.
