Blind and Mute Mufti: Valid if Communication Achieved
Expansion probe on the qualification of blind and mute muftis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for a blind or mute person serving as mufti and evaluate the dispute regarding the sign of a speaking mufti.

Core Idea
Both blind and mute persons can be muftis if they can write or use understood signs—but the sign of a speaking person is debated within the school.

This lesson examines the conditions of sight and speech for a mufti. The Hanafi school is lenient: a blind person can give fatwa if he writes or his sign is understood. Similarly, a mute person can issue fatwas through writing or signs, though a mute cannot serve as a judge. A key intra-Hanafi dispute concerns whether a speaking mufti may use signs; the relied-upon view allows it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and rulings
Identify the two types of impairments (blind and mute) and the ruling that they may give fatwa under certain conditions.
The condition is writing or understood sign.
2
Locate the condition
The condition for a blind or mute mufti is that he can either write or convey his ruling through an understood sign.
The text says: 'إذا كتب أو فهمت إشارته'.
3
Note the distinction between ifta' and qada'
The text contrasts ifta' with qada': a mute cannot be a judge even with signs because qada' requires a specific verbal formula.
The judge must pronounce 'حكمت' or 'ألزمت' after a valid claim.
4
Analyse the dispute on the sign of a speaking mufti
Some Hanafi scholars (e.g., al-Marghinani) reject the sign of a speaking mufti because speech is possible and signs are ambiguous. Others accept it.
The relied-upon opinion (from al-Quniyah, al-Nawazil) accepts the sign.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The mufta bihi is that the sign of a speaking mufti is valid and the mustafti may act upon it.
This is based on the purpose of conveying the ruling being achieved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آداب الفتوى للإمام النووى
٢٢
والأعمى، والأخرس (1) إذا كتب ، أو فهمت إشارته .
١٠ - قال الشيخ أبو عمرو بن الصلاح وينبغي أن يكون كالراوي في
لمن لا تقبل شهادته له . (رد المحتار : ٤٦٨) (سعيد أحمد)
(۱) قوله : "والأخرس وفى تلقيح المحبوبي : أن الإشارة من المفتى الناطق يعمل بها، فلا يختص بالأخرس " . وفى "القنية رامزاً لعين الأئمة المكي : إشارة المفتى برأسه مكان قوله : "نعم"، وللمستفتى أن يعمل به " . ورمز للنوازل عن أبي القاسم مثله، ورمز لظهير الدين المرغيناني : "لا ؛ لأن إشارة الناطق لا تعتبر " .
(البحر : ٢٦٣/٦) وفي الدر : لا حريته وذكورته ونطقه، فيصح إفتاء الأخرس لا قضاؤه . ويكتفى بالإشارة منه لا من القاضي ؛ للزوم صيغة مخصوصة كـ"حكمت" و "ألزمت بعد
دعوى صحيحة .
وأما الأطرش : وهو من يسمع الصوت القوى، فالأصح الصحة بخلاف الأصم ، وينبغي أن يكون الحكم كذلك في المفتي . فإن قلت : قد يفرق بينهما بأن المفتى يقرأ صورة الاستفتاء، ويكتب جوابه، فلايحتاج
إلى السماع . قلت : الظاهر من كلامهم عدم الاكتفاء بهذا فى القاضي مع أنه يمكن أن يكتب له جواب الخصمين، فكذا فى المفتى. ويمكن الفرق بأن القضاء لا بد له من صيغة مخصوصة بعد دعوى صحيحة، فيحتاط فيه بخلاف الإفتاء؛ فإنه إفادة الحكم الشرعي ولو بالإشارة، فلا يشترط فيه السماع. (المنح ملخصاً) قلت : لا شك أنه إذا كتب له وأجاب عنه، جاز العمل بفتواه. وأما إذا كان منصوباً للفتوى يأتيه عامة الناس، ويسألونه من نساء، وأعراب، وغيرهم، فلا بد أن يكون صحيح السمع ؛ لأنه لا يمكن كل سائل أن يكتب له سؤاله . وقد يحضر إليه : الخصمان ، ويتكلّم أحدهما بما يكون فيه الحق عليه لا له ، والمفتى لم يسمع ذلك منه ، فيفتيه على ما سمع من بعض كلامه فيضيع حق خصمه، وهذا قد شاهدته كثيراً، فلا ينبغي التردد في أنه لا يصلح أن يكون مفتيًا عاما ينتظر القاضي جوابه؛ ليحكم به، فإن ضرر مثل هذا أعظم من نفعه . والله سبحانه أعلم. (رد المحتار: ٤/ ٤٦٨) (سعيد أحمد)
Etiquette of Fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi. 22. And the blind, and the mute: if he writes or his sign is understood. 10. Shaykh Abu 'Amr ibn al-Salah said: And it is appropriate that he be like the narrator for one whose testimony is not accepted for him. (Radd al-Muhtar: 468) (Sa'id Ahmad) (1) His saying: "and the mute" — and in Talqih al-Mahbubi: that the sign from a speaking mufti is acted upon, so it is not exclusive to the mute. And in al-Quniya: the mufti's sign with his head takes the place of his saying "yes", and the mustafti may act upon it. And he signified from al-Nawazil from Abu al-Qasim similar to it, and from Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani: "No, because the sign of a speaking person is not considered." (al-Bahr: 6/263) And in al-Durr: his freedom, masculinity, and speech are not conditions, so the mute's ifta' is valid but not his qada'. His sign suffices, unlike the judge, because a specific formula like "I have judged" and "I have obligated" after a valid claim is required. As for the hard of hearing (atrash), who hears loud sounds: the soundest opinion is its validity, unlike the deaf (asamm), and the ruling should be the same for the mufti. If you say: A distinction can be made between them: the mufti reads the written question and writes his answer, so he does not need to hear. I say: The apparent from their words is that this is insufficient for the judge even though he can have the opponents' answers written for him, so likewise for the mufti. A distinction can be made: judgment necessarily requires a specific formula after a valid claim, so caution is exercised, unlike ifta', which is conveying a legal ruling even by sign, so hearing is not a condition. (al-Minh, summarized) I say: There is no doubt that if he has it written for him and he answers, acting on his fatwa is permissible. But if he is appointed for general fatwa and common people come to him, women, Bedouins, and others, he must have good hearing, because not every questioner can write his question. And occasionally both opponents come to him, and one of them speaks what is actually against him, not for him, and the mufti does not hear that from him, so he issues a fatwa based on part of his speech, thus wasting the right of his opponent. I have witnessed this often, so there should be no hesitation that he is not fit to be a general mufti whose answer the judge awaits to rule by, for the harm of such a person is greater than his benefit. And Allah knows best. (Radd al-Muhtar: 4/468) (Sa'id Ahmad)
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the physical conditions required of a mufti within the Hanafi school. The author states that a blind person and a mute person can give fatwa if they can write or if their understood sign is used. This is based on the principle that ifta' is communication of a legal ruling, which can be achieved through writing or sign. The commentary includes a disagreement about the sign of a speaking person (i.e., a mufti who can speak but chooses to use a sign): some scholars accept it, others do not. The distinction between ifta' and qada' (judgment) is highlighted: qada' requires a specific verbal formula, so a mute cannot be a judge even with signs, whereas ifta' is less formal. The main discussion focuses on hearing: the hard-of-hearing (atrash) can be a mufti according to the soundest opinion, but the completely deaf (asamm) cannot, because the mufti needs to hear the question. However, the author brings a practical distinction: if the mufti is a private scholar who receives written questions and responds in writing, hearing is not essential. But if he is a public mufti appointed for the general populace, he must have good hearing, because many people cannot write their questions, and there is a risk of miscarried justice. The author emphasizes that the harm of a deaf mufti in such a role outweighs the benefit, and such an appointment is invalid. This ruling is a rasm rule about the qualification of the mufti, with practical considerations based on custom (urf) and public interest (maslahah).

Key Terms
الأعمى (al-aʿmā): A blind person; the text states that a blind person can give fatwa if he writes or has his sign understood.
الأخرس (al-akhras): A mute person; the text says he can give fatwa if he writes or his understood sign is used.
الإشارة (al-ishārah): Sign or gesture used to convey a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon opinion in the text, can a speaking mufti use a head nod to indicate 'yes' as a valid fatwa?
A) No, because he can speak.
B) Yes, because the sign is clear and the mustafti may act on it.
C) No, only a mute mufti can use signs.
D) Yes, but only if he also writes it down.

Key Takeaway
A blind or mute mufti can issue valid fatwas as long as the ruling is conveyed clearly through writing or an understood sign; the sign of a speaking mufti is also accepted by the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Hearing-Impaired Mufti: Distinction Between Private and Public Fatwa
Expansion probe on hearing conditions for mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a hard-of-hearing (atrash) and a completely deaf (asamm) mufti, and apply the rule that a general mufti must have good hearing.

Core Idea
A hard-of-hearing mufti is valid; a deaf mufti can only serve if he receives written questions, but he is unfit for general public fatwa.

This lesson addresses the hearing capacity of a mufti. The text distinguishes between the hard-of-hearing (atrash) and the completely deaf (asamm). The soundest opinion permits a hard-of-hearing mufti, but a deaf mufti is problematic, especially for a public appointment. The author brings practical considerations: a deaf mufti cannot fully hear questions and may cause injustice. However, if the mufti operates through written correspondence, hearing is not necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of hearing impairment
The text distinguishes 'atrash' (hard-of-hearing, hears loud sounds) and 'asamm' (completely deaf).
The ruling differs for each.
2
Determine the ruling for each category
For atrash: the soundest opinion (asahh) is that he may be a mufti. For asamm: he is excluded from being a general mufti to prevent harm.
The text says 'فالأصح الصحة' for atrash and 'بخلاف الأصم'.
3
Understand the distinction between general and private fatwa
If the mufti receives written questions and responds in writing, hearing is not essential; but a public mufti must hear questions from a diverse populace.
The author provides a practical example: women and Bedouins cannot always write.
4
Analyse the harm of a deaf public mufti
A deaf mufti may only hear part of a dispute, leading to unjust fatwas. The author says 'ضيع حق خصمه' (wastes the opponent's right).
The harm outweighs the benefit.
5
Synthesise the final ruling
A deaf person cannot be a general mufti appointed for the public, but may serve privately using written communication.
The text ends with 'لا يصلح أن يكون مفتيًا عاما'.

Etiquette of Fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi. 22. And the blind, and the mute: if he writes or his sign is understood. 10. Shaykh Abu 'Amr ibn al-Salah said: And it is appropriate that he be like the narrator for one whose testimony is not accepted for him. (Radd al-Muhtar: 468) (Sa'id Ahmad) (1) His saying: "and the mute" — and in Talqih al-Mahbubi: that the sign from a speaking mufti is acted upon, so it is not exclusive to the mute. And in al-Quniya: the mufti's sign with his head takes the place of his saying "yes", and the mustafti may act upon it. And he signified from al-Nawazil from Abu al-Qasim similar to it, and from Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani: "No, because the sign of a speaking person is not considered." (al-Bahr: 6/263) And in al-Durr: his freedom, masculinity, and speech are not conditions, so the mute's ifta' is valid but not his qada'. His sign suffices, unlike the judge, because a specific formula like "I have judged" and "I have obligated" after a valid claim is required. As for the hard of hearing (atrash), who hears loud sounds: the soundest opinion is its validity, unlike the deaf (asamm), and the ruling should be the same for the mufti. If you say: A distinction can be made between them: the mufti reads the written question and writes his answer, so he does not need to hear. I say: The apparent from their words is that this is insufficient for the judge even though he can have the opponents' answers written for him, so likewise for the mufti. A distinction can be made: judgment necessarily requires a specific formula after a valid claim, so caution is exercised, unlike ifta', which is conveying a legal ruling even by sign, so hearing is not a condition. (al-Minh, summarized) I say: There is no doubt that if he has it written for him and he answers, acting on his fatwa is permissible. But if he is appointed for general fatwa and common people come to him, women, Bedouins, and others, he must have good hearing, because not every questioner can write his question. And occasionally both opponents come to him, and one of them speaks what is actually against him, not for him, and the mufti does not hear that from him, so he issues a fatwa based on part of his speech, thus wasting the right of his opponent. I have witnessed this often, so there should be no hesitation that he is not fit to be a general mufti whose answer the judge awaits to rule by, for the harm of such a person is greater than his benefit. Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the physical conditions required of a mufti within the Hanafi school. The author states that a blind person and a mute person can give fatwa if they can write or if their understood sign is used. This is based on the principle that ifta' is communication of a legal ruling, which can be achieved through writing or sign. The commentary includes a disagreement about the sign of a speaking person (i.e., a mufti who can speak but chooses to use a sign): some scholars accept it, others do not. The distinction between ifta' and qada' (judgment) is highlighted: qada' requires a specific verbal formula, so a mute cannot be a judge even with signs, whereas ifta' is less formal. The main discussion focuses on hearing: the hard-of-hearing (atrash) can be a mufti according to the soundest opinion, but the completely deaf (asamm) cannot, because the mufti needs to hear the question. However, the author brings a practical distinction: if the mufti is a private scholar who receives written questions and responds in writing, hearing is not essential. But if he is a public mufti appointed for the general populace, he must have good hearing, because many people cannot write their questions, and there is a risk of miscarried justice. The author emphasizes that the harm of a deaf mufti in such a role outweighs the benefit, and such an appointment is invalid. This ruling is a rasm rule about the qualification of the mufti, with practical considerations based on custom (urf) and public interest (maslahah).

Key Terms
الأطرش (al-aṭrash): A hard-of-hearing person who can hear loud sounds; the soundest opinion permits him to be a mufti.
الأصم (al-aṣamm): A completely deaf person; the author excludes him from being a general mufti due to harm.
الضرر (al-ḍarar): Harm; the decisive factor that prevents a deaf mufti from being a general mufti.

Key Takeaway
A hard-of-hearing person can be a mufti; a completely deaf person cannot be a general mufti due to practical harm, but can serve if all communication is in writing.

The Mufti is Like a Narrator, Not a Witness
Statement that a mufti is like a narrator (rawi) and not a witness (shahid).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the mufti is analogous to a narrator (rawi) rather than a witness (shahid) and identify the practical implications for fatwa issuance.

Core Idea
The mufti is a conveyor of general Shariah, not a personal witness — his fatwa is untainted by bias.

How should a mufti handle personal relationships when issuing fatwas? This lesson unpacks a foundational rule of Hanafi iftaa: the mufti is like a narrator of hadith, not a witness in court. Understanding this distinction clarifies why a mufti may give fatwas even to close relatives, and why his role is broader than that of a judge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on personal factors
Note that the passage states kinship, enmity, benefit, and harm do not affect the mufti. This is the core ruling.
2
Understand the analogy to the narrator
The mufti is like a narrator (rawi) because he reports a general legal ruling from the Shariah without personal specification.
Riwāyah (narration) is general; shahādah (testimony) is specific.
3
Contrast with the witness and judge
A witness testifies about a specific event and thus is subject to suspicion of partiality. A judge’s ruling is binding (ilzām), so impartiality is required.
4
Derive the practical consequence
The mufti may issue a fatwa even for someone for whom he would not be an acceptable witness or judge.
E.g., the mufti can give a fatwa to his son.
5
Note the broader principle
Fatwa is broader than judgment and testimony because it lacks compulsion and is general in nature.
This expands the mufti's accessibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آداب الفتوى للإمام النووى (۱) ۲۳ أنه لا يؤثر (1) فيه قرابة وعداوة، وجرّ نفع ، ودفع ضر؛ لأن المفتي في حكم مخبر عن الشرع بما لا اختصاص له بشخص، فكان كالراوى لا كالشاهد (۲)، وفتواه لا يرتبط بها إلزام ، بخلاف حكم القاضي . (۱) قوله : "لا يؤثر " كذا في "البحر" عن "شرح الروض" . (سعيد أحمد) (۲) قوله : فكان كالراوى لا كالشاهد فيجوز للمفتى أن يفتى من لا تقبل شهادته له وإن لم يجز أن يشهد أو يقضى له، والفرق بينهما أن الإفتاء يجرى مجرى الرواية فكأنه حكم عام بمنزلة إخبار العباد عما شرعه ربهم لهم، بخلاف الشهادة والحكم فإنه يخص المشهود له والمحكوم له فيتطرق التهمة إليهما . والحاصل أن الفتيا أوسع من الحكم والشهادة.
Etiquette of Fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi (1) 23: That kinship, enmity, drawing benefit, or repelling harm do not affect him, because the mufti is in the position of an informant about the Shariah with no special connection to a person, so he is like the narrator, not like the witness, and his fatwa is not linked to compulsion, unlike the judge's ruling. (1) His saying: "does not affect" — thus in al-Bahr from Sharh al-Rawd. (Sa'id Ahmad) (2) His saying: "so he is like the narrator, not like the witness" — thus it is permissible for the mufti to give fatwa to one whose testimony is not acceptable for him, even if he may not testify or judge for him. The difference between them is that ifta' runs in the course of narration, as if it is a general ruling in the manner of informing the servants of what their Lord has legislated for them, unlike testimony and judgment, which are specific to the one testified for and the one judged for, so suspicion of partiality arises. The conclusion is that fatwa is broader than judgment and testimony.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule about the mufti's impartiality. The author states that a mufti should not be affected by personal relationships (kinship, enmity, personal gain or loss) because his role is to inform about the Shariah, which is general and not specific to any person. Therefore, he is like a narrator (rawi) who transmits hadith, not like a witness (shahid) who testifies in a specific case. The consequence is that a mufti may give a fatwa even to someone for whom he would not be an acceptable witness (e.g., his close relative), but he may not testify or judge for that person. The commentary explains the reasoning: ifta' is similar to narration (riwaya) as it is a general ruling, while testimony and judgment are specific and subject to suspicion of partiality. The legal effect is that fatwa is less restricted than judgment and testimony, allowing the mufti to serve a wider range of people without disqualification due to personal connections.

Key Terms
الراوي (al-rāwī): The narrator of hadith; the mufti is likened to him because both convey general reports about the Shariah without personal stake.
الشاهد (al-shāhid): A witness in court; the mufti is unlike him because the witness testifies to a specific event and is thus subject to suspicion of partiality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the mufti's fatwa is binding like a judge's ruling.
B) Because the mufti is like a narrator of hadith, conveying general legal rulings without personal specification.
C) Because the mufti is not required to be just.
D) Because a witness is not allowed to testify for a relative, but the mufti is allowed.

Key Takeaway
A mufti is like a narrator of hadith, not a witness; personal relationships do not disqualify him from issuing fatwas.

The Fasiq's Fatwa: Consensus and Dissent
Consensus that a fasiq's fatwa is invalid with discussion of disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a fasiq's fatwa is invalid and identify the relied-upon opinion from the internal dispute.

Core Idea
A fasiq's fatwa is invalid by consensus; the rare contrary view is rejected due to risk of corruption.

This lesson examines the qualification of a mufti's moral uprightness (adalah). The source passage presents a consensus that a fasiq (perpetrator of major sins) cannot issue a valid fatwa, while also addressing a minority opinion within the Hanafi school that allowed it. Understanding this rule is essential for preserving the integrity of religious edicts.

Guided Path
1
State the consensus claim
Recall that al-Khatib transmitted ijma (consensus) that a fasiq's fatwa is invalid. This is the starting point of the passage.
Ijma is a primary source of Islamic law.
2
Identify the opposing view
Note that some Hanafi scholars, like the author of al-Kanz, argued that a fasiq can be a mufti because he will strive to avoid error.
This view is based on the hope of ijtihad and caution against mistakes.
3
Analyze the later critique
Examine the rebuttal in Radd al-Muhtar: the reasoning of al-Kanz is not evident in contemporary times, as a fasiq may deliberately distort texts for corrupt purposes.
This critique reflects the principle of sadd al-dhara'i (blocking pretexts).
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Conclude that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that a fasiq's fatwa is not relied upon at all, as affirmed by the investigator Ibn Abidin.
This is the operative rule for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٢ - واتفقوا على أن الفاسق لا تصح فتواه، ونقل الخطيب فيه إجماع - (۵) قوله : واتفقوا على أن الفاسق لا تصح فتواه" قال في التنوير وشرحه : " والفاسق لا يصلح (مفتيًا لأن الفتوى من أمور الدين والفاسق لا يقبل قوله في الديانات ، واختاره كثير من المتأخرين وجزم به صاحب المجمع في متنه، وهو قول الأئمة الثلاثة أيضا. وظاهر ما في التحرير أنه لا يحل استفتاؤه اتفاقاً . وقيل : نعم يصلح، وبه جزم في الكنز ؛ لأنه يجتهد حذار نسبة الخطأ وفى رد المحتار: قوله : "لأنه يجتهد إلخ " هذا التعليل لا يظهر في زماننا ؛ لأنه قد يعرض عن النص الضرورى قصداً لغرض فاسد، وربما عورض بالنص فيدعى فساد النص. ثم قال المحقق الشامي والحاصل أنه لا يعتمد على فتوى المفتى الفاسق مطلقا
12. And they agreed that the fatwa of a fasiq is not valid, and al-Khatib transmitted consensus on it. (5) His saying: "they agreed that the fatwa of a fasiq is not valid" — said in al-Tanwir and its commentary: "And the fasiq is not fit to be a mufti because fatwa is among matters of religion, and the fasiq's statement is not accepted in religious matters. And many later scholars chose this, and the author of al-Majma' affirmed it in his matn, and it is also the saying of the three Imams. And the apparent from al-Tahrir is that consulting him is not permissible by agreement. And it was said: Yes, he is fit, and this was affirmed by the author of al-Kanz, because he strives to avoid attribution of error. And in Radd al-Muhtar: His saying 'because he strives, etc.' — this reasoning is not evident in our time, for he may deliberately turn away from the necessary text for a corrupt purpose, and perhaps he may object to the text claiming it is corrupt. Then the investigator al-Shami said: The conclusion is that the fatwa of a fasiq mufti is not relied upon absolutely.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule about the qualification of the mufti regarding piety (adalah). The author states that there is consensus among scholars (including the Hanafi school and the other three Imams) that a fasiq (a sinful person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins) cannot give a valid fatwa. The commentary notes that this is the relied-upon position of many later Hanafi scholars, as in al-Majma'. However, there is an opposing view (attributed to the author of al-Kanz) that a fasiq can be a mufti because he will strive to avoid error. The commentator (from Radd al-Muhtar) rejects this reasoning in contemporary times, arguing that a fasiq may deliberately ignore clear texts for corrupt purposes. Therefore, the final ruling is that a fasiq mufti's fatwa is not to be relied upon at all. This rule is essential to maintain the integrity of religious edicts; fatwa is a religious matter, and only upright persons can be trusted.

Key Terms
الفاسق (al-fāsiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, lacking moral uprightness (adalah).
الإجماع (al-ijmāʿ): Consensus of scholars; al-Khatib transmitted consensus that a fasiq's fatwa is not valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His fatwa is valid if he is knowledgeable.
B) His fatwa is not valid, and one should not consult him.
C) His fatwa is valid only for others, not for himself.
D) He can give fatwa but it is makruh.

Key Takeaway
A fasiq's fatwa is invalid by consensus; the Hanafi relied-upon position rejects any exception due to the danger of religious corruption.

Fatwa of the Mastur (One Whose Apparent Justice is Known)
Establishing the ruling on the fatwa of a mastur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two views on the fatwa of a mastur and state the preferred Hanafi position, explaining the reasoning and the analogy to testimony.

Core Idea
A mastur's outward justice suffices for fatwa because inner justice is difficult for non-judges to verify.

This passage from the commentary on Rasm al-Mufti addresses a common practical question: can a mufti whose inner piety is unknown but outwardly just issue fatwa? The Hanafi school presents two views, and the more correct one permits it, distinguishing the role of a mufti from that of a judge. Understanding this ruling helps future muftis know when they can rely on their own and others' fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Define 'mastur' linguistically and legally
The mastur is a person whose outward conduct appears just (adl) but whose inner state of piety has not been tested. Legally, this term has specific implications for fatwa and testimony.
Derived from the root 's-t-r' meaning to cover; hence 'one whose inner state is covered/unknown'.
2
Identify the two views
First view (asahh): permissibility of his fatwa. Second view: impermissibility, analogizing to testimony.
The author states 'asahh' indicating the more correct view is permissibility.
3
Understand the proof for permissibility
The reasoning: inner justice (al-adalah al-batinah) is difficult for non-judges to verify, so reliance on outward signs is sufficient for fatwa.
This is a practical consideration (istihsan) due to necessity.
4
Understand the opposing proof
The opposing argument: analogy to testimony (shahadah), where a mastur is not accepted as a witness, so his fatwa should also be rejected.
The text mentions that the disagreement is similar to the dispute on marriage witnessed by mastur witnesses.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a mastur mufti's fatwa is valid and binding for the questioner, though some may advise caution. The mirrorto the khilaf in marriage with mastur witnesses.
The disagreement is internal to the Hanafi school, not with other schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٣ - وأما المستور : وهو الذى ظاهره العدالة ولم تختبر عدالته باطنا ، ففيه وجهان أصحهما جواز فتواه ؛ لأن العدالة الباطنة يعسر معرفتها على غير القضاة، والثاني : لا يجوز كالشهادة و الخلاف كالخلاف في صحة النكاح بحضور المستورين.
13 - As for the mastur: he is the one whose apparent justice is known but whose inner justice has not been tested. There are two views, the more correct of which is the permissibility of his fatwa, because inner justice is difficult to verify for other than judges. The second view: it is not permissible, like testimony, and the disagreement is like the disagreement about the validity of marriage in the presence of mastur witnesses.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the permissibility of issuing fatwa by a 'mastur'—a person whose outward justice (adalah) is apparent but whose inner state of piety has not been thoroughly examined. The text presents two opinions within the Hanafi school. The preferred view (asahh) allows his fatwa, reasoning that inner justice is not easily known except by qadis who have the authority to investigate. The second view analogizes to testimony (shahadah), where a mastur is generally not accepted as a witness, and thus extends that to fatwa. The disagreement is similar to the difference regarding the validity of a marriage contract witnessed by mastur witnesses. The Hanafi position in fatwa is to adopt the first view, as it is easier and more practical, considering that the primary duty of a mufti is to issue legal answers based on apparent circumstances.

Key Terms
المستور (al-mastur): A person whose outward conduct appears just (adl) but whose inner state of piety has not been verified
العدالة الباطنة (al-adalah al-batinah): Inner justice; the hidden state of piety and moral rectitude that only Allah knows fully

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because his inner justice is assumed to be perfect
B) Because verifying inner justice is difficult for non-judges
C) Because he is equivalent to a qadi in authority
D) Because the majority of scholars permit it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi iftaa, a mastur mufti is permitted to issue fatwa because outward justice suffices; verifying inner justice is not required for non-judges.

Judge Issuing Fatwa: Permissibility and Conditions
Rule on a judge issuing fatwa with intra-madhhab ikhtilaf and reconciliation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a judge issuing fatwa and apply the restriction that he may not give fatwa to a litigant in a pending case.

Core Idea
A judge may give fatwa, but not to a litigant in a case before him, to avoid the appearance of bias.

This lesson examines a nuanced issue in Hanafi legal practice: may a judge also act as a mufti? The well-known position permits it, but there is an important qualification to preserve judicial integrity. We will explore the reasoning behind the two views, the concept of 'place of suspicion' (mawdi' tuhmah), and the reconciliation that yields the operative ruling for a judge issuing fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling
Identify the default Hanafi position: a judge is like any qualified person and may give fatwa without dislike. This is the well-known (mashhur) view.
This is the starting point; exceptions apply.
2
Identify the two views on judicial matters
Within the madhhab, there are two views regarding fatwa in matters related to qada: one permits (because the judge is qualified), the other prohibits (because it is a place of suspicion). Understand the basis of each.
The ikhtilaf is intra-madhhab, not between schools.
3
Understand why suspicion (tuhmah) arises
Explain the reasoning: the mufti bases fatwa on diyanah (religious obligation), while the judge bases judgment on zahir (outward evidence). If a judge gives a fatwa and later a similar case comes before him, he might judge contrary to his fatwa, leading to allegations of inconsistency. Trace the example of Shuraih.
This is the core concern: preserving the integrity of the judiciary.
4
Learn the reconciliation
Later Hanafi scholars reconciled the two views by distinguishing the addressee: a judge may give fatwa to anyone who is not a litigant in his court, but he should not give fatwa to a litigant in a case brought to him.
This is the mufta bihi position, affirmed in al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
5
Apply the rule
Given a scenario, determine whether the judge may issue a fatwa. For example, a judge is asked a general question by a person not involved in any case before him — he may answer. If the person is a litigant in a pending case, he should not answer.
The key variable is whether the person is a litigant in a case before the judge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٥ - والقاضي كغيره في جواز الفتيا بلا كراهة، هذا هو الصحيح المشهور من مذهبنا . قال الشيخ : ورأيت في بعض تعاليق الشيخ أبي حامد أن له الفتوى فى العبادات، وما لا يتعلّق بالقضاء. وفي القضاء وجهان لأصحابنا: أحدهما: الجواز ؛ لأنه أهل والثاني : لا ؛ لأنه موضع تهمة .
(۳) قوله : "لأنه موضع تهمة " فيكون لكلام الناس عليه مجالا ، ولأحد الخصمين عليه مقالا، وهذا لأنه ربما يقع الفرق بين الفتوى والقضاء؛ لأن المفتي يفتي بالديانة، والقاضى يقضى بالظاهر ، ولأن القاضى إذا راعي الحجج والوثائق والبينات، يثبت الحكم عنده بخلاف ما أفتى به ، فإذا أفتى بشيء ثم وقع الخصام في مثله واحتكموا إليه وقضى بخلاف ما أفتى اتخذ ذلك طعنا عليه، ويكون هو صادقا في فتواه وقضاءه كليهما . من أجل ذلك قال شريح: "أنا أقضى لكم ولا أفتى " . ومن أجل ذلك استثنى
وقال ابن المنذر : تكره الفتوى فى مسائل الأحكام الشرعية ) ، وقال شريح: أنا أقضى ولا أفتى.
(۱) قوله : " الشرعية وفي نسخة : بإسقاط "الشرعية " . (سعيد أحمد)
ووفق بعضهم بين القولين، ففى البحر عن الظهيرية : " ولا بأس للقاضي أن يفتي من لم يخاصم إليه، ولا يفتى أحد الخصمين فيما خوصم إليه " . وفي كافي الحاكم : وأكره للقاضي أن يفتى فى القضاء للخصوم كراهة أن يعلم خصمه قوله فيتحرز منه بالباطل" . فهذا كما ترى جمع بين القولين بحمل قول الجواز على الإفتاء لغير الخصم، وعدم الجواز على الإفتاء للخصم . وأقره فى الدر المختار فراجعه : (٥)
15 - The judge is like others in the permissibility of giving fatwa without dislike; this is the correct and well-known position in our madhhab. Al-Shaykh said: I saw in some comments of Abu Hamid that he may give fatwa in matters of worship (ibadat) and what does not relate to judicial matters (qada). Regarding judicial matters, there are two views among our companions: one permits it because he is qualified, and the second does not because it is a place of suspicion.
Footnote (3): His statement 'because it is a place of suspicion' means that people's talk would have scope against him, and one of the litigants would have a point of objection. This is because the difference between fatwa and qada can occur: the mufti gives fatwa based on religious obligation (diyanah), while the judge judges based on outward appearances (zahir). If the judge considered the evidence, documents, and witnesses, he would establish a ruling contrary to his fatwa. Thus, if he gives a fatwa on a matter and then a similar dispute arises and he judges contrary to his fatwa, that would be taken as a defect against him, though he is truthful in both his fatwa and his judgment. For this reason, Shuraih said: 'I judge for you and do not give fatwa.' And for this reason, an exception was made.
And Ibn al-Mundhir said: Fatwa in matters of legal rulings (ahkam shar'iyyah) is disliked. And Shuraih said: I judge and do not give fatwa.
Footnote (1): His statement 'al-shar'iyyah' and in another copy it is omitted. (Sa'id Ahmad)
Some reconciled the two views: In al-Bahr from al-Zahiriyyah: 'There is no harm for a judge to give fatwa to one who does not bring a case to him, and he should not give fatwa to one of the two litigants regarding what is brought to him.' And in Kafi al-Hakim: 'I dislike for the judge to give fatwa in judicial matters for litigants, out of dislike that his opponent might learn his statement and protect himself with falsehood.' So this, as you see, is a combination of the two views, carrying the permission on fatwa to a non-litigant and the non-permission on fatwa to the litigant. And he affirmed it in al-Durr al-Mukhtar, so refer to it.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of a judge (qadi) issuing fatwa. The well-known Hanafi position is that a judge may issue fatwa without any dislike, just like any other qualified person. However, there is a nuanced discussion. Some scholars, like Abu Hamid, restrict the judge to fatwa in matters of worship (ibadat) and matters unrelated to his judicial duties; for matters that fall under qada (judicial rulings), there is disagreement. The two views within the madhhab are: (1) permission because the judge is qualified, and (2) prohibition because it is a place of suspicion (tuhmah). The footnote explains the reason for suspicion: if a judge gives a fatwa, and later a similar case comes before him, he might judge contrary to his fatwa, leading people to question his consistency. The example of Shuraih, who refrained altogether from fatwa to avoid such suspicion, is cited. The later scholars reconciled the two positions by distinguishing the addressee: the judge may give fatwa to someone who is not a litigant in his court, but should not give fatwa to a litigant in a case before him. This is supported by references from al-Bahr and Kafi al-Hakim, and affirmed in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. Thus, the judge's fatwa is permissible with this practical restriction to avoid the appearance of bias.

Key Terms
القاضي (al-qadi): The judge appointed to adjudicate disputes
موضع تهمة (mawdi' tuhmah): A place of suspicion; situation that may cast doubt on the judge's impartiality
الديانة (al-diyanah): Religious obligation; the inward dimension of a ruling that the mufti considers

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciliation in the Hanafi school, when is a judge prohibited from issuing a fatwa?
A) Whenever the fatwa concerns judicial matters
B) When the person asking is a litigant in a case before that judge
C) When the judge has already judged a similar case
D) When the fatwa is about matters of worship

Key Takeaway
A judge may issue fatwa, but he must avoid giving it to a litigant in a pending case to prevent any suspicion of bias.

The Hierarchy of Muftis: Independent and Dependent
Classification of muftis into independent and dependent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five categories of muftis in the Hanafi tradition and describe the condition of the independent mufti.

Core Idea
Muftis are either independent (mustaqill) or non-independent; independent muftis are extinct, and all modern fatwa comes from four subcategories.

Welcome to this lesson on the classification of muftis. This passage from Abu 'Amr lays out the foundational hierarchy that governs who can issue fatwa in the Hanafi school. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for gauging the authority of any fatwa you encounter and knowing the limits of a mufti's ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the two primary categories
Identify that muftis are primarily divided into independent (mustaqill) and non-independent (ghayr mustaqill). The independent mufti builds fiqh upon his own principles outside established madhhab rules.
The independent level is considered extinct per al-Suyuti.
2
Memorize the four subcategories of non-independent muftis
From the footnote, learn the four types: 1) absolute non-independent (mujtahid muntasib), 2) restricted mujtahid (mujtahid muqayyad), 3) people of tarjih (ahl al-tarjih), 4) people of fatwa (ahl al-fatya).
Each subcategory has decreasing scope of authority.
3
Note the condition for the independent mufti
The independent mufti must be a master (qayyim/faqih). This condition, along with the requirement of full ijtihad, makes this rank virtually unattainable in later times.
The text also mentions that fatwa in worship and customs was allowed due to lack of disputes.

Source Text

Chapter on the Categories of Muftis
16 - Abu 'Amr said: Muftis are of two types: independent (mustaqill) and other.
17 - The first type: the independent, its condition along with what we have mentioned is that he be a master (qayyim). The proponents of this view (allowed) his fatwa in matters of worship and customs, because no disputes occur in them.
Footnote (2): His statement 'independent and other' - know that the muftis according to the author are five types: because either he is independent or other. The independent is the absolute mujtahid who is called the mujtahid in the law. The non-independent is of four categories: 1. the absolute mujtahid who is not independent, sometimes called the affiliated mujtahid (mujtahid muntasib). 2. the restricted mujtahid (mujtahid muqayyad), sometimes called the mujtahid in specific issues. 3. the people of tarjih (preponderance). 4. the people of fatwa (ahl al-fatya).
Footnote (3): His statement 'the independent' is the one who independently establishes his own principles on which he builds fiqh, separate from the established principles of the madhhabs. Al-Suyuti said: 'This is something that has been lost for ages; even if one desired it today, it would be impossible.' More will be said about this.
Footnote (4): His statement 'qayyim' and in another copy 'faqih' instead of 'qayyim'. (Sa'id Ahmad)
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of muftis according to Abu 'Amr, who divides them into two main categories: mustaqill (independent) and ghayr mustaqill (non-independent). The independent mujtahid is one who possesses the ability to derive rulings directly from the primary sources without adhering to a specific madhhab. The footnote from al-Suyuti notes that such a level has become extremely rare. The non-independent are further subdivided into four types as explained in footnote (2): the absolute mujtahid who is not fully independent (mujtahid muntasib), the restricted mujtahid (mujtahid muqayyad) who can only derive rulings within a specific area, the people of tarjih (who weigh evidences within a madhhab), and the people of fatwa (who merely transmit rulings). The condition for the independent mufti is that he be 'qayyim' (a master) or 'faqih' (jurist). The text also mentions that the proponents of this view allowed fatwa in matters of worship and customs because no disputes occur. This classification is foundational for understanding the hierarchy of muftis in the Hanafi tradition.

Key Terms
المستقل (al-mustaqill): The independent mujtahid who builds fiqh upon his own principles outside the established madhhab rules
المجتهد المطلق (al-mujtahid al-mutlaq): The absolute mujtahid capable of independent derivation
أهل الترجيح (ahl al-tarjih): Those qualified to prefer one opinion over another within a madhhab
أهل الفتيا (ahl al-fatya): Those who transmit the rulings of the madhhab without the ability to weigh or derive

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The mufti hierarchy ranges from independent mujtahid (extinct) to simple transmitter of rulings; each level has its scope and authority in issuing fatwa.

The Independent Mufti: Definition and Conditions
Definition and conditions of the fully independent mujtahid mufti.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of the independent mufti (al-mufti al-mustaqill), list his required conditions, and explain why he fulfills the communal obligation (fard kifaya).

Core Idea
The independent mufti is a mujtahid mutlaq who derives rulings from primary sources, fulfilling a communal obligation.

In the Hanafi tradition, muftis are categorized by their level of independence. This lesson examines the highest rank: the mustaqill, who does not rely on any madhhab. Understanding these conditions is crucial for recognizing the qualifications required for true ijtihad and the rarity of such scholars in later centuries.

Guided Path
1
Classify the Mufti Types
The opening statement divides muftis into two categories: independent (mustaqill) and non-independent. Recognize that this passage focuses on the first type.
The second type (non-independent) is discussed later in the text.
2
Extract the Core Definition
The independent mufti is defined as one who stands on evidence without taqlid of any madhhab. He is both a mufti and a mujtahid mutlaq.
3
List the Required Sciences
The passage enumerates conditions: knowledge of legal proofs (Quran, Sunnah, ijma, qiyas), usul al-fiqh, Arabic sciences (grammar, morphology, lexicology), abrogation (nasikh/mansukh), and scholarly agreements/disagreements.
These are not merely recommended but prerequisite conditions (shurut).
4
Identify the Practical Requirement
Beyond theoretical knowledge, the mustaqill must have practical skill (darba wa irtiyad) in applying the tools of derivation.
This distinguishes mere memorization from true ijtihad.
5
Note the Communal Obligation
The passage states that such a mufti fulfills the fard kifaya. Understand that the existence of qualified muftis is a collective duty.
If no mustaqill exists, the obligation shifts to lower ranks.

Section on the divisions of muftis. 16 - Abu 'Amr said: The muftis are two types: independent and other. 17 - The first type: the independent. Its condition, along with what we have mentioned, is that he be knowledgeable in the evidences of legal rulings from the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and analogy, and what is annexed to them in detail; and this has been detailed in fiqh books and thus made easy—praise be to Allah. And that he be aware of the conditions required in the evidences, the aspects of their indications, and how to derive rulings from them—this is learned from usul al-fiqh. He must know the sciences of the Qur'an, hadith, abrogating and abrogated, grammar, lexicography, morphology, and the differences and agreements of scholars to the extent that he can fulfill the conditions of the evidences and derive from them. He must have practice and habituation in using that, know fiqh, and have mastery over its major issues and details. Whoever gathers these descriptions is the absolute independent mufti, through whom the communal obligation is fulfilled, and he is the absolute independent mujtahid because he stands independently on evidence without taqlid or adherence to anyone's madhhab. Commentary
This passage from the section on divisions of muftis classifies muftis into two broad categories: independent (mustaqill) and non-independent. The independent mufti is the absolute mujtahid who derives rulings directly from primary sources without following any madhhab. The author then lists the extensive conditions for such a mufti: mastery of legal proofs from Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and qiyas; knowledge of usul al-fiqh and how to extract rulings; proficiency in related sciences like Arabic grammar, nasikh and mansukh, and scholarly consensus and disagreement; and practical experience in applying these tools. This knowledge must be comprehensive enough to enable him to derive rulings independently. Such a mufti fulfills the communal obligation (fard kifaya). The text ends with a note from Abu 'Amr that the condition of memorizing fiqh issues specifically may not be required for the independent mufti, though the passage breaks off.

Key Terms
مستقل (mustaqill): An independent mufti who relies on his own ijtihad without taqlid of any madhhab.
فرض الكفاية (fard al-kifaya): A communal obligation that if some perform it, others are relieved of responsibility.

Ijtihad for the Mufti: Condition for Fard Kifaya
Clarifying the requirement of ijtihad for the mufti who performs fard kifaya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct condition of ijtihad for a mufti discharging fard kifaya and distinguish it from the independent mujtahid.

Core Idea
Ijtihad is required for the fard-kifaya mufti, but not for the independent mujtahid – a reconciliation of two views.

The qualifications of a mufti have been debated among scholars. One key issue is whether ijtihad – the ability to derive rulings – is a prerequisite for issuing fatwas. This passage presents a nuanced resolution: it is necessary for the mufti who takes on the communal obligation, but not for the absolute mujtahid. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The passage addresses whether ijtihad (the ability to derive legal rulings) is a condition (shart) for being a mufti.
Two earlier opinions are mentioned: one rejecting it because fiqh is the fruit of ijtihad; another making it a condition.
2
Analyze the two earlier opinions
Some scholars (e.g., those of many famous books) argue ijtihad cannot be a condition because fiqh (the outcome) would then precede its condition. Others, like Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi, insist it is a condition.
The first view is based on a logical chronological argument; the second emphasizes the importance of capability.
3
Recognize the third opinion
The author presents a third, reconciliatory view: ijtihad is a condition for the mufti who fulfills the communal obligation (fard kifaya), but not for the independent mujtahid.
This is described as 'like arbitration' (tahkim) between the two earlier positions.
4
Understand the rationale
The mufti discharging fard kifaya must be able to perceive rulings of incidents quickly and without great difficulty. That ability is attained only through memorizing the chapters of fiqh and its issues, which is a form of operational ijtihad.
This practical necessity makes the condition valid for this category of mufti.
5
Distinguish from the independent mujtahid
The independent mujtahid (al-mujtahid al-mustaqill) establishes the school and does not require this condition because his ijtihad is of a higher order. The mufti at the level of fard kifaya is a capable follower who can produce rulings from the school's methodology.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the hierarchy of muftis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كثير من الكتب المشهورة ؛ لكونه ليس شرطاً لمنصب الاجتهاد ؛ لأن الفقه ثمرته فيتأخر عنه ، وشرط الشيء لا يتأخر عنه . وشرطه الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الأسفراييني وصاحبه أبو منصور البغدادي وغيرهما . واشتراطه فى المفتى (۱) الذي يتأدّى به فرض الكفاية هو الصحيح، وإن لم يكن كذلك في المجتهد المستقل. قوله : واشتراطه فى المفتى هذا قول ثالث، وهو بمنزلة التحكيم بين القولين السابقين. وقوله : "هو الصحيح " لأن حال المفتى يقتضى اشتراط كونه على صفة يسهل عليه معها إدراك أحكام الوقائع على القرب من غير تعب كبير . وهذا لا يحصل لأحد من الخلق إلا بحفظ أبواب الفقه ومسائله .
Many of the famous books [consider it not a condition for the position of ijtihad] because fiqh is its fruit and thus comes after it, and the condition of a thing cannot be after it. The teacher Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and his companion Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi and others made it a condition. And making it a condition for the mufti who fulfills the fard kifaya is correct, even if not so for the independent mujtahid. His statement: 'And making it a condition for the mufti' – this is a third opinion, and it is like arbitration between the two previous opinions. And his statement: 'it is correct' because the state of the mufti necessitates that he be in a condition that makes it easy for him to perceive the rulings of incidents quickly without great effort. This is not attained by anyone except by memorizing the chapters of fiqh and its issues.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage discusses a key rasm al-mufti rule: whether ijtihad (the ability to derive rulings) is a condition for the mufti. Some scholars said it is not a condition because fiqh (jurisprudence) is the outcome of ijtihad, and a condition cannot be after the conditioned thing. However, Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi held that it is a condition. The author (Imam al-Nawawi) presents a third view that reconciles these: ijtihad is required for the mufti who discharges the communal obligation (fard kifaya), meaning he must be able to derive rulings when needed, but this does not apply to the independent mujtahid who creates the school. The commentary explains that this condition is practical: the mufti must have the ability to access rulings without excessive difficulty, which requires memorization of fiqh chapters and issues. This ruling is foundational for understanding the qualifications of a mufti in the Hanafi tradition.

Key Terms
المفتى (al-mufti): The one who issues fatwas; in this context, specifically the mufti who fulfills the communal obligation (fard kifaya).
فرض الكفاية (fard kifaya): A communal obligation; if some perform it, others are absolved; here, issuing fatwas for the community.
الاجتهاد (ijtihad): The ability to derive legal rulings from the sources; here, operational ijtihad through memorization of fiqh and its issues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is absolutely required for all muftis.
B) It is required only for the mufti who fulfills fard kifaya.
C) It is not required at all.
D) It is required only for independent mujtahids.

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad is a required condition for the mufti who discharges the communal obligation (fard kifaya), but not for the independent mujtahid.

The Mufti's Knowledge Requirement: Memorize Most, Derive the Rest
Rasm al-Mufti rule on sufficient knowledge for fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum knowledge requirement for a mufti in the Hanafi tradition and identify the authority level of this rule.

Core Idea
A mufti need not memorize every ruling; mastering the majority and deriving the rest suffices.

This lesson examines a foundational principle from Rasm al-Mufti: the mufti is not expected to have every single fiqh ruling committed to memory. Instead, a practical and attainable standard is set — he must have memorized the majority of rulings and possess the ability to derive the remaining ones quickly. This rule protects the mufti from an impossible burden and ensures the continuity of fatwa issuance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the first line of the passage. What does it say about the mufti's memory? It states that it is not required (لا يشترط) that all rulings be in his mind. Instead, a sufficient condition (يكفيه) is that he is a memorizer of most (حافظا المعظم).
2
Understand the Standard
The passage specifies two components: (1) he must be a hafidh of the mu'azzam (the majority), and (2) he must be able to comprehend the rest quickly (متمكنا من إدراك الباقى على قرب). This is the threshold for competence.
The term 'mu'azzam' (most) is left intentionally flexible; it indicates a significant majority, not a precise fraction.
3
Analyze the Textual Variant
The commentary notes a variant from Ibn al-Salah: 'hafidhan li al-mu'azzam' (memorizer of most) with a different grammatical construction. Both versions confirm the same meaning. Note that the author of the matn (likely al-Nawawi) uses 'hafidha al-mu'azzam'.
Such variants are common in classical texts and do not change the legal import.
4
Apply to Hanafi Iftaa
In the Hanafi tradition, this rule corresponds to the concept of a mujtahid within the madhhab. The mufti must have mastered the major issues (especially from Zahir al-Riwayah) and be able to derive subsidiary rulings via qiyas and other principles.
This standard is less stringent than that of a mujtahid mutlaq but more rigorous than a mere narrator of fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This is the nineteenth point in Imam al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa. It establishes a practical rasm rule for the mufti: he need not have every single ruling memorized. Instead, he must have memorized the majority of rulings in fiqh and have the capability to derive the remaining rulings swiftly when needed. The commentary notes a slight variant in Ibn al-Salah's text, confirming the same meaning. In the Hanafi tradition, this aligns with the concept of being a mujtahid within the madhhab, where one has mastery of the major issues and can derive subsidiary rulings from the principles. This rule guards against the impossible expectation of total memorization.

Key Terms
حافظ المعظم (hafidh al-mu'azzam): One who has memorized the majority of fiqh rulings; not necessarily every detail.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A mufti qualifies by memorizing the majority of rulings (especially Zahir al-Riwayah) and possessing the ability to derive the rest swiftly.

Knowledge of Arithmetic Required for a Mufti
Stating a condition: mufti must know arithmetic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi condition that a mufti must know arithmetic, apply it to inheritance calculations, and evaluate the ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars.

Core Idea
A mufti must master arithmetic to avoid fatal inheritance errors.

The mufti's qualifications extend beyond fiqh knowledge. This lesson addresses a practical condition: arithmetic. The passage from 'Uqood Rasm al-Mufti discusses whether a mufti must know mathematics to solve fiqhi problems. The correct view is that it is required, because errors in calculations can invalidate fatwas, especially in the precise field of inheritance (farāʾiḍ). We will explore the dispute and why the requirement is upheld.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Read the passage and locate the condition being discussed: knowledge of arithmetic (ḥisāb). What problem does it solve? Note that the condition is specifically for 'fiqhi mathematical problems'—these are not abstract math problems but ones arising in fiqh rulings.
2
Understand the ikhtilaf
The author reports that Abu Ishaq and Abu Mansur recorded a dispute among Hanafi scholars. Who are these scholars? (They are early Hanafi authorities.) What was the disagreement? Some said arithmetic is not required; others said it is.
The opposing view may have considered it sufficient to consult experts.
3
Apply the tarjih
The author states that the correct view (al-aṣaḥḥ) is to require it. Why? Consider the thamarah al-khilaf: a mufti ignorant of arithmetic could issue an incorrect fatwa in inheritance, where shares must sum correctly. The tarjih is based on preventing harm.
4
Map to fatwa hierarchy
In the Hanafi fatwa system, this condition is part of the mufti's qualifications (rasm al-mufti). It is a condition for the mufti himself, not necessarily for every fatwa. The mufta bihi is that a mufti must know enough arithmetic to solve the mathematical problems that arise in his fatwas.
5
Test with an example
Consider an inheritance problem: a husband dies leaving a wife, a daughter, and a father. The shares are: wife 1/8, daughter 1/2, father 1/6 as a Quranic heir, plus the remainder as asaba. The base number is 24. Without arithmetic, a mufti might miscalculate. This shows why arithmetic is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٠ - وهل يشترط أن يعرف من الحساب ما يصحح به المسائل الحسابية الفقهية؟ حكى أبو إسحاق وأبو منصور فيه خلافا لأصحابنا، والأصح اشتراطه .
20. And is it required that he know from arithmetic that by which he can correct fiqhi mathematical problems? Abu Ishaq and Abu Mansur reported a dispute among our companions about it, and the correct (view) is to require it.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This short passage addresses a specific condition for the mufti: knowledge of arithmetic (ḥisāb). The context is that many fiqh issues, especially inheritance (farāʾiḍ), involve numerical calculations. The author notes that Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi reported a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars on whether this is required. The author then declares the correct (al-aṣaḥḥ) view to be that a mufti must know enough arithmetic to correctly handle such problems. In Hanafi practice, this is essential for a mufti issuing fatwas on inheritance, as mistakes in calculations can lead to incorrect distributions.

Key Terms
الحساب (al-ḥisāb): Arithmetic; knowledge of numbers and calculations needed for fiqhi problems like inheritance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is required.
D) It is required only for inheritance specialists.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must know arithmetic because fatwas involving calculations, especially inheritance, require precision; the correct view makes this a condition.

Tajzi' al-Ijtihad: Permissibility of Specialization for the Mufti
A ruling on the permissibility of partial ijtihad for muftis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on tajzi' al-ijtihad and evaluate the arguments for and against specialization in one fiqh chapter.

Core Idea
A mufti may specialize in one chapter—his fatwa carries the same weight as an absolute mujtahid's in that scope.

This passage from Rasm al-Mufti tackles a crucial question: Must a mufti master all of fiqh, or can he limit himself to one chapter like inheritance? The answer shapes how we train and credential muftis today. We will examine three scholarly positions and the reasoning behind the Hanafi reliance on absolute permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Extract the three views on tajzi' al-ijtihad mentioned in the passage: (1) absolute permission (al-Ghazali, Ibn Burhan), (2) absolute prohibition (some scholars), (3) permission only for inheritance (Ibn al-Sabbagh).
Note the author declares the correct view (al-aṣaḥḥ) as absolute permission.
2
Understand the basis of prohibition
The opposing view argues that ignorance of other chapters may hide counter-evidence. Write down the interconnection argument clearly.
Interconnection: e.g., marriage, divorce, and inheritance are linked.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response counters that the specialized mufti is assumed to know all evidences of his chapter; the interconnection is weak for self-contained chapters like inheritance.
Note the emphasis on definitive (qaṭʿī) proofs in inheritance.
4
Recognize the tarjih mechanism
Identify why absolute permission is mufta bihi: the specialized mufti has exerted effort (ijtihad) within his domain, so his ruling is like that of an absolute mujtahid in that scope.
This is a practical concession for continued fatwa operation.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
Consider a mufti who only handles inheritance (farāʾiḍ) questions. According to the correct view, his fatwas are valid. Why is this important for institutional fatwa councils?
This allows specialization in complex fields like Islamic finance or medical ethics.

(۱) قوله : والأصح جوازه مطلقا لأنه قد عرف الحق بدليله، وقد بذل جهده في معرفة الصواب ؛ فحكمه في ذلك حكم المجتهد المطلق فى سائر الأنواع . وحجة المنع : تعلق أبواب الشرع وأحكامه بعضها ببعض، فالجهل ببعضها مظنة للتقصير في الباب والنوع الذى قد عرفه ولا يخفى الارتباط بين كتاب النكاح والطلاق والعدة وكتاب الفرائض، وكذلك الارتباط بين كتاب الجهاد وما يتعلق به وكتاب الحدود والأقضية والأحكام ، وكذلك عامة أبواب الفقه . و من فرق بين الفرائض وغيرها رأى انقطاع أحكام قسمة المواريث ومعرفة الفروض ومعرفة مستحقيها عن كتاب البيوع والإجارات والرهون وغيرها ؛ لأن الفرائض لا تبنى على غيرها من الأحكام، فأما ما عداها من الأحكام فبعضه مرتبط ببعض وأيضًا فإن عامة أحكام المواريث قطعية، وهي منصوص عليها في الكتاب والسنة .
21. Then we only require the mentioned sciences to be combined in an absolute mufti in all chapters of the Sharia. As for a mufti in a specific chapter, like rituals and inheritances, it suffices him to know that chapter. This is what al-Ghazali and his companion Ibn Burhan (with fatha on ba) and others decisively stated. And among them are those who absolutely prohibited it (3), and Ibn al-Sabbagh allowed it only in inheritances, and the correct (view) is that it is allowed absolutely (1). His statement: 'And among them are those who absolutely prohibited it' – i.e., they prohibited partial ijtihad, where some people acquire ijtihad power in some chapters like inheritance by knowing its evidences through induction from himself or from a complete mujtahid and then examining them. The proof of this view is that it is possible that in what he does not know of the evidences, there is a counter-argument to what he knows, unlike one who encompasses all and examines it. The response: this is very far-fetched because the assumption is that he knows all the evidences of that chapter. His statement: 'And the correct (view) is that it is allowed absolutely' because he has known the truth by its evidence and has exerted his effort in knowing the correct; thus his ruling in that (chapter) is like the ruling of an absolute mujtahid in other types. The proof of prohibition is the interconnection of the chapters of Sharia and their rulings; ignorance of some is a cause of deficiency in the chapter and type he has known, and the connection between the book of marriage, divorce, waiting period, and the book of inheritances is not hidden, as well as the connection between the book of jihad and what relates to it and the book of hudud, judgments, and rulings, and likewise most chapters of fiqh. And whoever distinguished between inheritances and others saw that the rulings of division of estates, knowledge of shares, and knowledge of their recipients are disconnected from the book of sales, rentals, pledges, and others; because inheritances are not built on other rulings. As for other rulings, some are connected to others. Also, most rulings of inheritance are definitive, textually prescribed in the Quran and Sunnah.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of tajzi' al-ijtihad (partial ijtihad) in the context of mufti qualifications. The author presents three positions. The first, held by al-Ghazali and Ibn Burhan, is that for a mufti specialized in one chapter (like rituals or inheritance), it suffices to know only that chapter. The second position absolutely prohibits specialization, arguing that Sharia chapters are interconnected and partial knowledge may lead to error due to missing counter-evidence from other chapters. The third, from Ibn al-Sabbagh, allows it only for inheritance. The author declares the correct view (al-aṣaḥḥ) to be that specialization is absolutely permissible. The commentary provides the reasoning: one who has exerted effort in a chapter knows its evidences fully, so his ruling is like that of a complete mujtahid within that scope. The opposing argument of interconnection is countered by noting that inheritance rulings are largely self-contained and definitive, not dependent on other chapters. In Hanafi usul, this ruling allows a mufti to specialize, especially in farāʾiḍ, without needing to be a master of all fiqh.

Key Terms
تجزئ الاجتهاد (tajzi' al-ijtihad): The concept that ijtihad can be partial, confined to a specific chapter or issue.
مفت مطلق (mufti mutlaq): An absolute mufti qualified to issue fatwas in all chapters of Sharia.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in the passage, can a mufti specialize in only inheritance (farāʾiḍ) and issue fatwas?
A) No, he must know all chapters.
B) Yes, absolutely.
C) Yes, but only if he also knows sales and rentals.
D) Only if he is an independent mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
A mufti specialized in a self-contained chapter like inheritance can issue valid fatwas; absolute ijtihad is not required.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

The Mujtahid Muntaqil: The First State of the Affiliated Mufti
Discourse on the hierarchy of muftis and the first state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the mujtahid muntaqil from a muqallid and explain the significance of madhhab affiliation without blind taqlid.

Core Idea
A mufti can be affiliated to a madhhab while exercising ijtihad, following the imam's method, not his every fatwa.

For centuries, the independent mujtahid has been absent, and fatwa has been entrusted to those affiliated with the four imams. This passage from Sharh Uqood Rasm al-Mufti outlines the four states of such a mufti, beginning with the highest: the mujtahid muntaqil. Understanding this state is crucial for grasping the Hanafi hierarchy of muftis and the nature of taqlid versus ijtihad within a madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four states
The author lists four states for the affiliated mufti. The first is that he is not a muqallid of his imam. This sets the stage for the discussion.
The other three states are not elaborated here.
2
Define the mujtahid muntaqil
The mujtahid muntaqil possesses all conditions of ijtihad but does not innovate his own principles; he follows the method (tariqa) of a specific imam. This is not blind taqlid.
Examples include Abu Yusuf and Muhammad from the Hanafis.
3
Analyze the dispute: muqallid or mujtahid?
There is a dispute among scholars whether senior followers like Abu Yusuf and al-Muzani were muqallids or independent. The author presents arguments from both sides.
The key evidence: their fatwas often disagree with the imam.
4
Understand the author's position
The author supports the view that these followers were not muqallids in everything. They followed the imam's method because they found it the soundest, not by blind imitation.
This is supported by al-Shafi'i's own prohibition of taqlid.
5
Apply the distinction
Recognize that a mufti who is a mujtahid muntaqil can issue fatwas based on inference within the madhhab, and his authority is higher than a mere muqallid.
This affects how we evaluate fatwas and the weight of different opinions.

Etiquette of issuing fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi: its permissibility absolutely (1) 28 (2) 22 - Second category: the mufti who is not independent, and for a long time there has been a lack of the independent mufti, and fatwa has shifted to those affiliated with the followed imams of the schools. The affiliated mufti has four states: 23 - First: that he is not a muqallid of his imam (3), neither in the school nor in the evidence. (1) His statement: 'The correct view is its permissibility absolutely because he has known the truth through its evidence and has exerted his effort in knowing correctness; so his ruling in that is like the ruling of the absolute mujtahid in all categories.' The argument of prohibition: the connection of the chapters of Sharia and its rulings with one another, so ignorance of some is a source of deficiency in the chapter and category that he has known, and the connection between the book of marriage, divorce, and waiting period, and the book of inheritance is not hidden, similarly the connection between the book of jihad and what pertains to it, and the book of hudud, judiciary, and rulings, and likewise all chapters of fiqh. Whoever distinguishes between inheritance and others sees that the rulings of estate division, knowledge of prescribed shares, and knowledge of those entitled to them are disconnected from the book of sales, leases, pledges, etc.; because inheritance is not built on other rulings. As for other rulings, some are connected to others. Also, most inheritance rulings are definitive, and they are stated in the Book and Sunnah. Reference: I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in 4/216, and Fatawa Ibn al-Salah, p. 28. (2) His statement: 'For a long time there has been a lack of the independent mufti' — and in al-Mizan by Imam Abdul Wahab al-Sha'rani: 'Jalal al-Suyuti narrated that absolute ijtihad is of two types: absolute non-affiliated as done by the four imams, and absolute affiliated as done by their senior companions. He said: No one claimed absolute non-affiliated ijtihad after the four imams except Imam Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari, and that was not conceded to him.' End. And in al-Mizan also: 'There is not in the ability of anyone after the four imams to innovate rulings and extract them from the Book and Sunnah as far as we know ever. Whoever claims that for someone, we say to him: extract for us something that no imam has previously extracted.' Reference: al-Nafi' al-Kabir li man Tala'a al-Jami' al-Saghir, pp. 14-15. (3) His statement: 'That he is not a muqallid of his imam' — and he is called the absolute non-independent mujtahid, who possesses the conditions of ijtihad that the independent mujtahid possesses, but he did not innovate his own principles; rather he followed the method of an imam among the imams of the schools in ijtihad. He is an absolute affiliate. [Page 357] Etiquette of fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi 29 his evidence; because he is described with the quality of the independent, and he is attributed [to an imam] because he follows his method in ijtihad. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq claimed this quality for our companions, and it was narrated about the companions of Malik, Ahmad, Dawud, and most Hanafis that they adopted the schools of their imams by way of taqlid. Then he said: The correct view, which the verifiers have adopted, is what our companions adopted, that they adopted the school of al-Shafi'i not by taqlid of him, but because they found his methods in ijtihad and qiyas to be the soundest methods, and they had no alternative but ijtihad, so they followed his method. They sought knowledge of rulings by the method of al-Shafi'i. And Abu Ali al-Sanji (with kasra on the important letter) mentioned something similar, saying: 'We followed al-Shafi'i to the exclusion of others because we found his statement the most preponderant and most just, not because we made taqlid of him.' I say: That which they both mentioned is in accordance with what al-Shafi'i commanded them, and then al-Muzani in the beginning of his Mukhtasar (2) and others with his statement: 'With his informing them of his prohibition of taqlid of him and of others.' Neither independent nor restricted; because he did not make taqlid of his imam, but he followed his method in ijtihad, like Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar from the Hanafis, Ibn al-Qasim and Ashhab from the Malikis, and al-Buwaiti and al-Za'farani from the Shafi'is. (1) His statement: 'This quality for our companions' — Shaykh Siddiq Hasan Khan said in Adab al-Mufti: 'This rank has been claimed from the Hanbalis by Qadi Abu Ya'la and Qadi Abu Ali ibn Abi Musa in his commentary on al-Irshad, and from the Shafi'is many. The Hanafis differed about Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl; the Shafi'is about al-Muzani, Ibn Surayj, Ibn al-Mundhir, and Muhammad ibn Nasr al-Marwazi; the Malikis about Ashhab, Ibn Abd al-Hakam, Ibn al-Qasim, and Ibn Wahb; and the Hanbalis about Abu Hamid and al-Qadi: were these independent in ijtihad or restricted by the schools of their imams? Whoever reflects on the conditions of these individuals, their fatwas, and their choices knows that they were not muqallids of their imams in everything they said, and their disagreement with them is too manifest to be denied, even if among them there were the independent and the prolific. The rank of these is below the rank of the imams in independence of ijtihad.' (2) His statement: 'Then al-Muzani in the beginning of his Mukhtasar' — Abu Ibrahim Ismail ibn Yahya al-Muzani said at the beginning of his Mukhtasar: 'I abridged this book from the knowledge of' (incomplete).
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Uqood Rasm al-Mufti discusses the second category of mufti: the affiliated mufti (al-mufti al-muntasib), who is not an independent mujtahid but belongs to a recognized madhhab. The author notes that for a long time, the independent mufti has been absent, and fatwa has devolved to those affiliated with the four imams. He then outlines four states of such a mufti, but only the first state is elaborated here. The first state is the mujtahid muntaqil (absolute affiliate): a mufti who possesses the qualifications of ijtihad but does not innovate his own principles; instead, he follows the method (tariqa) of a particular imam, such as al-Shafi'i. This is not blind taqlid, but adherence to the imam's methodology because it is found to be the soundest. The text then presents a dispute: al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq claimed that the companions of al-Shafi'i adopted his school not by taqlid but by conviction in his method, while others claimed they were mere muqallids. The author supports the former view, citing Abu Ali al-Sanji and the example of al-Muzani, who explicitly prohibited taqlid. The passage also discusses whether prominent disciples like Abu Yusuf and Muhammad from the Hanafis were independent mujtahids or restricted. The author concludes that they were not muqallids in everything, though their rank is below the imams. This is a crucial distinction for understanding the hierarchy of muftis and the nature of affiliation to a madhhab.

Key Terms
المفتي المنتسب (al-mufti al-muntasib): A mufti who is not an independent mujtahid but is affiliated to a specific madhhab, following its principles and methods.
المجتهد المطلق غير المستقل (al-mujtahid al-mutlaq ghayr al-mustaqill): A mujtahid who possesses all the conditions of independent ijtihad but does not create his own principles; he follows the method of a particular imam, such as al-Shafi'i or Abu Hanifa.
المقلد (al-muqallid): A mufti who follows his imam without personal investigation or evidence, simply adopting his rulings as authoritative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the first state of the affiliated mufti (al-mufti al-muntasib) from a mere muqallid?
A) He does not follow any imam.
B) He does not have the conditions of ijtihad.
C) He possesses the conditions of ijtihad but follows the method of a specific imam, not by taqlid but by conviction in his method.
D) He issues fatwas only in matters of inheritance.

Key Takeaway
A mufti affiliated to a madhhab can be a mujtahid muntaqil — possessing ijtihad qualifications and following the imam's method, not his every ruling — which elevates his fatwa above that of a mere muqallid.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
